O sons, if I have fulfilled this book for you, which is the last of the science of medicine, through its completion in me, and through its expositions and declarations by me, it seems to me that you will complete it with this treatise, which is part of the operation with the hand, and that is surgery.
For the operation with the hand is deprived in our time and age of all its strength, while the science of it is lost and its traces are left behind; and there remain of it only a few small descriptions in the books of the Ancients, which they have changed and directed to error and confusion, so that their understandings of it are closed off and its art is prolonged and stretched out.
And it seems to me that you will enliven this through the ordering of this treatise according to the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and so that you may come to the forms of the tools of cautery and of the other instruments of the work as is fitting, through addition of declaration and through preparation for it, which is necessary.
But the cause why there is no good artificer with his hand in our time is that the art of medicine is long, and that one has to be exercised before it in the science of anatomy, which was known to the Gauls as they knew the parts of the members and their forms; and the connection and separation of them; and had knowledge of the bones, the nerves, and the tendons, and the number of these, and their outgoing.
And for this Hypocras says that with a name is much, but in the work little, and most especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already said this in the introduction to this book; for whoever is not learned in what we have said of anatomy is evacuated and will fall into error by which men will die, as I have seen many of those, for some are formed in this science and throw themselves from it without experience.
For I saw a foolish and inexperienced physician make an ulcer of scrofula in the neck of a woman, and incise some arteries of the neck so that much blood flowed and the woman died under his hand.
And I saw another physician who had to swallow a stone of a man who had a process in his state; and the stone was large, and he came to him and pulled out that stone with a piece of the bladder, and the man on the third day died.
And I called to draw out that one, and saw that by the size of the stone and the disposition of the patient, who lay over him, he would die.
And I saw another physician who took stipends from a duke of our land for surgery, and treated a broken bone of a black person; and through his ignorance he drew out the fracture on the wound with poultices and bandages with strong tightness, and did not leave the wound to breathe.
And he drew it out according to his desires, and bound it up, and left it for several days, and commanded that we loosen the bandage; and he did this so that the leg and thigh became ulcerated, and came to harm.
And I was called to him, and prepared to loosen the bandage, and peace came to him, and he grew calm of his pains.
But indeed the corruption was confirmed in the member, so that I could not restrain it, and the corruption did not cease to wander in the member until the patient died.
And I saw another physician who made an ulcer of a malignant cancer, and it was ulcerated for several days, until the wickedness of it grew great with its lord.
The cause of this is that the malignant humour is pure in its nature; but it does not come to it through iron, unless there is a member which suffers from it altogether.
And for this, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation with the hand be considered in two ways; one is an operation associated with health, and the other an act with which are associated the greatest dispositions.
And I have already exhorted this in every place of this book in which occurs an operation in which there is error and fear; so that it is necessary for you to guard against this and leave that, so that the foolish do not obstruct your way and dissuade you.
And therefore take up your weapons with solicitude and with protection of God, and your patients with facility and firmness.
And use the best way through the grace of God, to lead to health and praiseworthy success; and lay aside the terrible illnesses of difficult cure, and take up your arms against what causes you fear, so that solicitude in your faith and in your name does not fail: for it will remain for your glory, and more in the world and beyond to your sanctities.
Gaulish already said in some of his own words: “Do not treat illnesses with evil, so that bad physicians are not named.”
And I have composed this book according to three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter ordered from the head into the feet, in the forms of the instruments and in the tools of cautery, and of what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of bleeding and piercing, and of cupping, and of bandages, and of extraction of thorns and their like, all chaptered and ordered according to the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and dislocation, and of cure for twisting and their like; a chapter ordered from the head into the feet, and of the forms of the instruments.
May I declare the quality of the parts of that cautery and the pains of it, and in what disposition cautery is prohibited.
Therefore I say that the word of the quality of the part of cautery and of the pain of it is long and prolix, and subtle through science, and secret hidden; and many wise men have spoken much of it, and they disagree on it, and for me a little of their words suffices, fearing prolixity.
And therefore I say that cautery brings forth universally in all complections, except two complecions, which are complictions of cold without matter.
In the compliction cold and dry with matter they disagree; for some say that cautery is beneficial to it, and others say the contrary of that, that cautery is not convenient in illness which is by quality and dryness, for the nature of fire is quality and dryness, and inconvenient that illness cold and dry is cauted and dry be healed.
And he says the contrary of that, that by cautery is made benefit in all illness cold and dry which one sees in the bodies of men; for when you compare the body of man and his humours to the compliction of fire, one feels the body of man hot.
And I say through the word of that, that I have discovered this experiment many times.
However, one should not come to this topic unless he is experienced and practiced in the chapter of cauteries through his knowledge of the diversity of human complexions and the dispositions of illnesses in their bodies, and the causes of those, and their accidents, and the space of time of those.
But in the other complexions, there is no fear of harm, especially with cold and damp illnesses; all doctors have agreed on this, and they are not disagreed upon when cauterization is done on them.
And know, children, that the secret of healing through cautery with fire and its goodness is that it is a simple action that only affects the part that is cauterized and does not affect any other part except with little harm.
But the action of a burning medicine spreads to the other parts of the body and makes it difficult to heal them and causes great harm.
But fire, through its subtlety and the goodness of its substance, only affects the part that is cauterized and not any other parts except with little harm. And we know this from experience and from a long time serving as a student in the study of the true causes; and so I am excused from speaking at length about it. And if it were not for the fact that I was not reading from a book, but rather a secret hidden in my mind, that the heat of fire and the quality of its action in the body and how one expels illnesses through it, demonstrated through philosophical reasoning, would be sharper in your minds.
And know, children, that they disagree on the time when it is convenient to do cauterization and stop, choosing the best time in springtime.
And I say that cauterization is convenient at all times because the harm caused by the accidental time is submerged in the benefit of it that is drawn from cauterization, especially if the cauterization is for pains or necessary wounds, strong and festering, which do not endure delay due to the fear of their coming, which is greater than the little harm caused by the timing.
And so do not fear in your hearts, o children, that this is not believed except by the common people and foolish doctors; it is that to that illness that is healed through cauterization, there is no reduction of time, and they pause the consequence of that.
And the cause is not as they think because cauterization is only in place of medicine that alters the complexion and dries the humidity which causes pain to come.
But cauterization surpasses medicine through the will of its strength and power of its action, and the greatness of its lord.
And it is possible that the illness returns at some time of the year, depending on the complexion of the patient and the location of their illness, and the strength of it, and what has been prepared in the body from the accumulation of excesses in it, and what has come to them through food intake and similar causes; but if the illness in which cauterization is administered is a subtle illness in a part with few excesses and humidity, just like tooth pain and its likes, then it is possible that the pain does not return, but this happens in small amounts.
But the common speech is that cauterization is the last resort of medicine; and this speech is true, not to say that they understand it, because they believe that it is not healing that benefits from medicine or any other cause except cauterization.
And the cause is in contrast to that; and the intention that cauterization is the last resort of medicine is only when we administer methods of healing on certain illnesses, and those medicines do not confer it, and especially at the end of the cause, they administer cauterization, and it benefits from that. And so from here we see that cauterization is the last resort of medicine, not according to the intention that the commoners and foolish doctors understand it.
And the ancients said that cauterization with gold is better than cauterization with iron; and they did not say this except for the equality of gold and subtlety of its substance.
And they said that the location of cauterization does not make a difference except when it is absolutely true; for we have already proven that it only makes a difference in some bodies without the others, and cauterization with gold is good and better than with iron just as they said; but when you do cauterization with fire with gold, it is not clear to you because it is heated according to the quantity that you want due to the thickness of the gold, and because it prepares to receive friction; and if you place it over the fire, some thin layers are formed so that the artisan can see through that.
And for this reason, cauterization with gold must be done more gently and closer to the operation.
And we have already ordered this chapter on cauterization according to chapters, and I have ordered those of the head between the feet, so that I may ask you what you want from it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization confers with humidity and coldness the servant accident of which there are two causes; it is a cause of soda and multitude of fluxes, called rhinitis, from the head to the parts of the ears and from the eyes flowing, and multitude of sleeping, and of tooth pain and of the gullet, and generally of all illnesses that come through coldness, just as paralysis, stroke, and their likes in those illnesses.
The form of this cauterization is as follows: first of all, command the patient to be evacuated with laxative medicine for three or four days, according to the strength of the patient and their age and habit.
And then command that they shave their head with a razor; and then let him lie between your legs on a low bed and let them place their hands on their feet.
And then pause the edge of your palm, then let it rest on the edge of the nose between your eyes. But when the place of your middle finger is reached, mark that place with a tent, and then light the cauterization olive, of which this is the form:
And then place it on the marked location, in such a way that it descends, and your hand be pressed little by little, and you revolve it.
And then lift your hand gently and look at the location; if you see that any amount of the head is uncovered with bone, just as with the amount of grain, then lift your hand and if it does not return with that iron, with another one, if it is cooled down, until that is seen by the eye what we have said to you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it on the location and let it be for three days.
And then place over it cotton soaked in oil and let it be over it until the char of the fire is destroyed. And then heal the location with tetramedication ointment once it has healed.
And so they say that when the Roman plaster becomes wet, as many times you throw a cloth over it, it is better and more beneficial.
And some of them say that the bone should be cauterized within the eye, and to have the cauterization within it until a part of the substance of the eye is destroyed.
And then scrape away what is dry from the bone, and then heal it.
And others say that with the cautery one must go to the end, until they press on the strong bones within which are found the bones, and this is the form of small alpecat. They think that from that place the vapors of the head expire.
And if the ulcer is open for a long time, it should be healed until it is consolidated.
But I have not seen these two species of cautery at all, except in some men according to the path of fear; and leaving that aside, for me it is better for health when it is; because the head is devitalized by solution of continuity in him just as we have testified in other members, and especially when the patient's head has a phlegmatic nature.
And the species of cautery first is more safe and better for me; and of that house and with that work I am saved.
Chapter second.
Of cautery of the head.
When in the whole head there occurs a chronic and long pain and this is prolonged in the patient, and he uses leeches and cupping glasses and caput medusae and poultices and cataplasms and bandages and cauteries and similar remedies, and especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery which we relate and does not benefit him at all from those causes, then consider that if the patient's head is of strong creation in its nature; and it is phlegmatic and finds great friction, then cauterize it after that with another cautery below that one a little. Then cauterize that above the horn of his head with a cautery until the scum is broken. The scum having been cut away and the quantity we have related discovered and cauterized, then cauterize that with a cautery in the rear part of the head in the place which is called a vessel — and that is the place in which the windsock is placed. And loosen your hand over it, and do not discover the bones, because by that the patient experiences great pain different from the pain of all other cauteries of the head.
And I say that this cautery is in its place, because the cautery with which you cauterize the horns of the head and the occipital is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle part of the head is cauterized; and this is its form.
Chapter third.
Of cauterization of not ancient migration.
When in the front part of the head there is a hard pain, or a stinging pain goes into the eye, then evacuate with medicated shampoos, and administer the other treatment which I have written about in the beginnings of illnesses; and if all does not benefit, then cauterization in it is in two ways: either cauterization with an acute burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is that you take a piece of alder wood and cut it up and mix the ends of it with both ends of the cautery. Then pierce the location of the pain in the temple with a pointed lance until it is in the place below the scum, and hidden.
Then bind it tightly over it with a descending constriction, and let that be for the space of three hours; and then loosen the ligature and treat it with water poured on it until the place makes a blister.
Then cure that with an ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the second treatise on burning medicines.
But the cauterization with iron is according to this manner: ignite the cautery of which this is the form:
and it is called a nail claw because its head has the form of a claw in which there is some curvature, and in the middle part of it is a small protuberance.
Then pause over the location of the pain, and hold your hand  — so that it is not heated by the iron which is below  — and turn the iron little by little with speed.
And if the quantity of it which is heated by the specificity of the scum as far as its depth, and raise your hand so that the artery is not heated which is below, so that there does not come a flow of blood.
Then moisten the cautery in salt water and pause it over the location, and let that be for three days; then pause a moistened cloth with vinegar on it, and cure it with an ointment until it is healed.
And if you wish, cauterize with the end of the scalpel according to cautery, and beware of incision of artery in this not ancient migration particularly.
Chapter fourth.
Of cauterization of ancient migration.
When you cure a migration with what we have said about the treatment preceding, and what we have said in the divisions of illnesses, and the treatment does not benefit, and you see for the illness that it does not suffice to it what we have said about the first cautery with medicine or with fire, then if you ignite the cautery cut into the scum until it is whitened, and when you have done this, then pause your hand over it for a while, and tighten it so that you cut the artery and it comes within to the bone.
However it is necessary for you to guard against the continuance of the mandible which is damaged because of mastication, and that the ligament not be heated and the nerve which hurts that, and that there not come from it a spasm.
And if it is over the custody and precaution of flow of blood from the artery that is cut, because in the cutting of it there is danger, and especially to him who ignores what he does and is not practiced and experienced; and let the operation be in him at the beginning.
And I will come with reminder of the regiment of flow of blood accidentally from the artery according to its way, in your location of this book.
And if you see of this illness that it does not suffice to that cautery and you see the patient's body tolerable, then cauterize that in the middle part of the head just as we have related, and cure the ulcer until it is healed.
And if you wish, administer the cautery which we have said in the chapter on stretching of artery with cautery having two ends, and that cauterization is better and more fitting.
Chapter fifth.
Of cauterization of pains of ears.
When in the ear there is pain from friction and it is cured with lacerative causes and with the other treatment which we have said in the division, and the pain is not alleviated, then ignite the cautery, and that is the one called puncta of which this is its form:
Then pause the cautery and let it be directed toward all of the ear just as the wheel is turned, or in the circuit of either eardrum if the pain is in one. Draw you out with the cautery of the edge of the ear little by little, so that you feel the location with your hand.
And if the cautery of many points in all the ears, and that is it, then cure the locations until they are healed.
Chapter sixth.
Of cauterization of torture.
Torture which is cured and has cauterization not but of the spices which come by phlegm, according to what we have said in the divisions of illnesses.
Avoid cauterization of the affected area due to dryness or nerve spasm.
When you treat this type of torture with blisters and scabs and wounds, and your cure is not effective, then you should cauterize the patient in three places: first, at the root of the ear; second, a little below his temple; and third, at the junction of two lips.
And apply your cautery opposite the affected area, for softening only occurs in the part that appears healthy.
And the way of cauterization is that you cauterize the ear at the highest point of its extremity, and on the temple another one, whose length should be according to the length of the fingernail; and apply your hand with the cautery until half of the size of a grain of cummin is burnt.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is made by the cutler, its shape being that of a table. But it is more subtle if you make it a little smaller; and one should cut away the excess thickness a little.
Then treat the area with what has been described before, until it is healed.
Chapter 7.
Of cauterization for ancient apoplexy.
When apoplexy is ancient, and you treat it as we have said, but your cure is not effective, and the patient has no fever, then cauterize him in four places: on all the corners of his head; with a cautery, also on the middle of his head, as we have said; and with a cautery on his neck. And the manner of the cautery is done as it has been said above.
And it is cauterized on the mouth of the stomach; and it is more effective behind; and it should be treated with what has been described.
Chapter 8.
Of cauterization for oblivion caused by a flame.
Let the patient be given at the beginning large blisters and pills of the purgative.
Then scrape the head all over, and apply to his neck a plaster of vinegar described in the treatise on plasters.
And apply it several times; for that is the way of cauterization. And do that according to the order which medicine has given, which I have already said.
And if it heals well because of that, but if not, then do to him three cauteries in the back part of his head, which descend from the highest part of the head to the lowest part of the neck. And let the distance between each cautery be the thickness of a fingernail, and treat the cautery with what it proceeds from.
But if you wish to increase the number and the patient can tolerate it, then do to him cauteries in the middle part; and if you wish to increase even more, then do them on the two corners.
Then treat that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-shaped according to the form which has been given above.
Chapter 9.
Of cauterization of parasites and of softening of all the body.
Let the patient be purged in his head with blisters and what we have said; then scrape the head of the patient, and cauterize him with a cautery on the middle part of his head, and with a cautery on all the corners of his head, and with a cautery on his neck, and with three cauteries on the back part of his neck.
And if it is necessary in the softening of the body's parasites, and the patients tolerate it, and the illness is strong, then cauterize him with four cauteries on the back part of his neck, and lastly with a cautery on the place where half of a grain of cummin has been burnt, and lift up your hand. Then treat that until it is healed; and let this be an olive-shaped cautery.
Chapter 10.
Of cauterization for epilepsy.
The epileptic should not be cauterized unless the epilepsy is caused by phlegm.
And so, let the brain of him be purged at the beginning with herbs and with the other cure which we have said in the division when the patient is large and tolerates the exception of medicines.
And if he is a child who does not tolerate medicines, then administer to him purgatives and masticables, before which for several days there should be an abstinence from food.
Then scrape the head of the patient, and cauterize him with a cautery on the middle part of his head according to the previous manner, and with another cautery on the back of his head, and on each corner of his head. And if the patient is strong and tolerates it, then cauterize him with cauteries which I have described, having paralysis and softening of the body on the back part of his neck and the back part of his back.
And let the cautery be olive-shaped according to what has preceded it.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a subtle cautery according to this manner.
Chapter 11.
Of cauterization for melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupted humours and gross phlegm, then cauterize him with cauteries which we have said to cause paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is an excess which inclines to melancholy, and the patient's body is moist, then give him something which purges his brain according to what has preceded in the division.
Then scrape the head of the patient, and properly make a bandage of linen over his skull, and apply that to the middle part of his head.
And let the patient be seated, and let him be held in every part.
Then apply liniment; and it is more worthy of old wine of sheep. And warm that over the fire with a moderate heat, the amount which he can tolerate when it is on him. Then put that on the middle part of his head in the circle, and let him lie down until it is cold.
And do that to the patient every day for a week with one application, while he keeps his other regimen until it is healed; or if you wish, cauterize him making small points according to his strength, unless you have your hand over a cautery thus ordered: this is a cautery which moistens the brain in equality.
Then apply that cloth steeped in wine or in woolen rags.
Chapter 12.
Of cauterization for water which descends into the hole.
When you have been told of the coming of water into the hole through the passages which you have described in the division, then begin and give to the patient something which purges his brain, and guard that from all moistness.
And let him sweat in a bath for several days.
Then command that he wash his head, and cauterize him with a cautery on the middle part of his head; and then cauterize him with two cauteries on two temples if there is the coming of water into two holes, or from one side if there is the coming of water from one hole.
Penetrate with a cautery all the veins and arteries which are under the skin; and let the cauteries be long in that length of the temples.
Be careful of bleeding when cauterizing; and if you see bleeding from him, stop it with some medicine which is possible for you.
And let there come to you wisdom, which is in extraction of arteries and abstention from those, and caution regarding bleeding.
And sometimes it is cauterized in the neck below the bones with two cauteries recently produced.
Chapter 13.
On cauterization of old tears.
When tears of the holes are old and thickly gathered, and through the veins and arteries which are in the manifest place of the head, and it is verified that this is superfluous, then cauterize that with a cautery which we have recounted in the chapter on the beginning of water falling into the holes, and with a cautery in the middle place of the head, and with two cauteries in the neck below the temples, and with two cauteries in the neck below the bones. And if there is a need for addition, then cauterize that in all parts of the hole of the eye above the extremity of the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter 14.
On cauterization for foul odor of the nose.
When you are treating someone with that which we have said in the division, and your treatment does not help, then prepare and give the patient enemas for three nights; then cut his hair, and cauterize him with a cautery made of olive oil. Then cauterize that with a cautery of clove on both eyebrows below the hair, one small cautery on each.
And be careful not to ignite the artery.
And this is the form of the cautery of clove:
Chapter 15.
On cauterization for softening of eyelids of the holes.
When the eyelid of the eye is made soft by illness or by moisture, then cauterize that eyelid with a cautery with this lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you treat it, cauterize that on both eyebrows a little with two cauteries all around, and let them be extended from the temples. Let the length of each cautery depend on the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press your hand with the cautery too much, but according to the amount that the upper part of the eyelid is adhered.
Chapter 16.
On cauterization for the eyelid of the eye when the hairs have turned to the parts inside and close the eye.
When the hairs of the eye have turned to the parts inside and close the eye, there are two ways to cauterize them: one way is with fire, and one way is with burning medicine.
If you cauterize him with fire, order the patient to open his eyes in such a way that he spreads out those hairs which are growing inwards, and let them be prolonged and curved; and if he closes his eye, then you strengthen his eyelids for him with a bandage so that he does not rub it while the hairs are growing.
Then when they are growing and curling, then put the head of the patient in your lap, and press on the eyelid of the eye with your finger, with a sign that is according to the shape of a dead leaf, and let the beginning of the sign be near the hairs.
Then put cotton dipped in egg white or in mullein tea on the eye.
Then heat up the cautery, of which this is the form:
Then cauterize over the figure which you have marked, little by little, in small spots, until the surface of the skin is burnt up, as though it were a leaf of myrtle; let it appear wholly as it should.
And a sign of health from your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted, and its hairs raised above the punctured place of the eye.
Then raise your hand and loosen it for three days, then put cotton on it with a bandage until the scar is healed. Then treat that with ointment until it is healed.
But if the hairs return for any cause, and the eyelid is made soft again, then do the cautery on that place again all over as you did at first.
But if the hairs are in the eyelid, then cauterize that until it is elevated to its natural place and is curved, and let the hairs not poke the eye.
But the cautery with burning medicine is that you order the patient to open his eyes in such a way that the hairs are prolonged and curved; then let the sign be made of linen according to the shape of a leaf of myrtle.
Then take of sage, one ounce, and of live coals, half an ounce. .I., Take that and mix it with honey, and make it into a poultice so that it is not cold; then spread that over the linen which is made according to the shape of a leaf of myrtle, and put that on one eye or both; then put cotton dipped in egg white on the eye, and let the head of the patient be in your lap.
Then hold the finger on the medicine and press that a little and do not rub it while the patient feels the biting of the medicine, for the biting is in him as though it were fire.
Then when he perceives that the biting has ceased, let him loosen the medicine and wash it off with water; then consider if you see the eyelid already lifted up as though it were washed after cauterization with fire or with incision, for then it is well; and if it returns to that of the medicine on the place in which the medicine did not take effect and is not blackened until your operation has been curved and the eye drawn together.
Then put a bandage on it so that the skin of the swelling is eradicated, and treat that with ointment of diascordium or another one until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you, after your operation to guard against the falling in of the medicine, and if the eyelid within a few days is made soft and some cause makes the hairs poke into the eye, then repeat your medicine on that place from which the eyelid is made soft, and altogether as you did at the beginning, and cover it so that it is healed.
And this is the form of a leaf of myrtle.
And know that the eyes of men are various according to magnitude and smallness, therefore according to that is made your contraction.
And do not be amazed at the way of rettitude for him who studies this art.
Chapter 17.
On cauterization for a fistula which is in the tear duct of the eye.
When you cure a fistula with that which we have said in the divisions of illnesses and your treatment does not confer, then know that you cauterize it according to this manner: order the patient to put his head in your lap, and let him hold his head by some servant in such a way that it is not rubbed or moved with his head.
Then put cotton dipped in egg white or in mullein tea on the eye of him.
Then heat up the cautery, of which this is the form:
Which is hollow, as it is shaped like a reed or a feather, from one extremity on which the cautery is made.
And if you wish, let it be penetrated into the other extremity. And if you want to let it come out like this with a tent.
But however, what is convenient is best for your work.
Then compress the fistula if it is open and draw out from him pus and dry that up. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the rottenness from him.
Then place upon him the cautery which should be well ignited and have your hand a little below the cautery at the start of the nose so that your hand does not err or the patient be disturbed, then place the cautery in the grommet and corrupt that.
And therefore if at the beginning of your cautery it pierces into the bone, that is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery once or twice, if necessary; and leave that as it is for three days, and then place upon that spot a plaster and surround it with an ointment which is drying until it is healed.
But if eleven days have passed and it is not healed, then place upon him an acute corrosive medicine so that the bone is uncovered and scrape according to what is convenient in his case.

Chapter 46.

Chapter 50.

And it is much more direct that it should be cauterized around it with a circle as we have said, or with many cauteries.

Chapter 53.
On cauterization of sores and similar maladies.
Many times one finds in the affected parts of a sick person a sore called a claw, and it is a serious, deep malady that penetrates the foot; and there are two methods of cauterizing them: with fire or with boiling water.
But cauterization with fire is done by lighting the cautery tool, which should be shaped like a vulture's beak and made of iron according to the size of the sore; its edges must be thin.
Then hold the lit cautery tool over the sore and, while doing so, move your hand in a circular motion around the sore until the cautery reaches the depth of the sore. Let this be done for three days until pus begins to discharge. Then apply a plaster made of crushed marsh mallows and salt over the sore for one night; this will pull out the roots of the malady.
And then treat the wound with generative ointment until it is healed.
And if it does not heal and often occurs on the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet, then you must administer cauterization with water to those sores; take a pipette made of bronze or iron or shaped like a vulture's beak, and hold it over the sore. Draw some boiling water into the pipette, close your hand around it, and move your hand in a circular motion around the sore so that the water penetrates the depth of the sore. Wait for an hour while the water bites into the sore; then remove the pipette, for the claw with its roots will have been pulled out by the boiling water.
And do this to all of them that appear on his body.
And then treat the sores from which the roots of the claws have been pulled with ointments that promote the growth of new flesh.

And his tremors cease and the sickness from cold is quickly healed.

Chapter 56.

Chapter six.

And let your effusion with which you pour the medicinal oils into the ear be with that instrument of which this is the form: And make that of silver or brass, narrow at the bottom and wide on top, in which there is a small hole, and flat on top.
And let it be as if it were in the cavity of the tube that contains what is necessary here. And whatever is put in the ear, let it be heated by fire a little. And beware that the cause which you distill in the ear is not too hot, for the medicines of the ear cannot endure that.
Chapter 7.
On the cure of pain in the ears.
Sometimes some children are born with pain in their ears, and that happens in some adults as well, opilacio in the middle of the ear by pus or by nothing at all in that spot; and this opilacio sometimes occurs in the middle of the ear canal which does not affect the eardrum, and sometimes it is on the outside.
And that which happens in the middle of it, which does not affect the eardrum, is in the most difficult dispositions for curing.

If its end is narrow and one side is sharp while the other is not, and the spatulum is of two edges so that it does not hurt the ear.

And if you have felt the swelling with water that the patient drinks easily, due to the difficulty of breathing, relieve his oppression easily; and beware lest you irritate the nerves of the ear.

Chapter 8.
Of the treatment of athezia, which occurs in the eyelids.
Athezia occurs in the eyelids due to some humidity, sometimes it is dry.
Therefore, you should have them cut with tweezers or a sharp knife, and remove them from their roots; and place the cut end of the piece on the affected area, using a pointed instrument.

And the best cauterization is when the cuts are made and the roots are pulled out, after some cause has returned them to their roots; and when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned and do not return.
Chapter 9.
The cure for granulation that occurs in the eyelids of people.
Often it occurs in the eyelids of people due to some cause which resembles granulation in its strength and hardness; and therefore granulation is called.
And it is an accumulation of thick humidity in the eyelid, both below and above.
Therefore, the operation on it is that you should consider whether the granulation comes from something that appeared under or above the eyelid and has spread everywhere; and the cause of it is ulceration.
Therefore, cut it open with a wide slit, and then scrape away the granulation all around with ease, until it is eliminated; and suspend it with a knife and excise it.
And if the size of it is large, then you should stitch it up; and cure it until it is healed.
And if it is small, you will not suffer harm from it because the eye's moisture restores it and incorporates it.
And if the granulation is declining towards the inner edges of the eyelid, then you should eviscerate the eyelid and lift up the granulation with a knife; cut away any other cause of it that you find there, and press down on it from all sides, and if the eyelid is elongated by the incision, then this will not be noticed in any way.
Then wash the eye with salted water, and cure the wound so that it incorporates into the skin until it is healed.
Chapter 10.
Of the cure of a cyst in the eyelid, above the eye.
A cyst is a grayle growth which is generated in the eyelids, above the eye, and mostly occurs in children; it damages the eyes of those who experience it, and they cannot bear to look at the brightness of the sun because tears are drawn towards them.
And so you will see them hanging down over their faces all the time, and sleeping over their faces; and so, under their eyebrows, the eyelids are moistened with cystic swelling, which appears as a protuberance in the eye; and when you press the spot with your finger, you feel that protuberance within.

And beware lest you cut into the slit with the cautery so that, when you use the cautery, you do not reach the eye and burn it.

But if an ulcer occurs on the spot, then cure it with cauterized plaster so that it is healed.

Chapter .xii.

Chapter 24.
Of the cure of a swelling in the nose.
When in the nose there are various swellings of which is some cause which is attached to scorpio of hair feet, or to polyps of hair; and this illness is called polypus because it is of the appearance of that hair represented. And from him there is swollen flesh, flaccid, of dusky color; and from him there is that is not leon not of dusky color.
Therefore in that which is in these flesh not fraudulent or cancerous, let there be the patient lying between your hands and exposed to the sun, and open his nose, and rub the eye of him in that flesh. And then extract that to the foramen, and then pierce with that which you have understood with a sharp stick from the foramen, and if when you have drawn it there is no cause from which the abscission is possible, then rub that with one of the subtle instruments with ease from which there is no cause. But if it oozes blood or pustules form, then let there be placed to him that which you have; or they are of the malignant pustules, then cut and cauterize that from which the blood has been discharged and all the cavities are completely open.
And then let there be placed in the nose also after the incision oil and water or vinegar. But if the nose is open and from it proceeds humidity into the palate, then know that it is healed.
But if the humidity does not penetrate according as it should, then you know that within him there is swollen flesh in the nose bones of which there has been no discharge.
Therefore let there be placed in the nose also after the incision oil of which there is some thickness, and rub in it hair of needles, and place between each joint the quantity of the finger or more, and open the sight and insert one end of the needle into the nose with the ray or other cause from which it is possible.
And once this is done, let them take their incense and hold it until it reaches the nostrils, and let it be burned over their throat; for many experienced soldiers do this action when they want to.
Then pause the two ends of the gallbladder; one end comes out through the nose and the other goes into the nose. Administer cauterization of flesh with the nails that are in the gallbladder, and do this until you taste that the flesh is cauterized by the nails.
Then remove the gallbladder and place in the nose a cauterization of blood, and do this for three days or more until the ointment corrode all that is rotten in the flesh.
And at the end of the cause, place a lead pipe in the nose for several days until it is healed.
But if the wound starts to bleed again, use that one. [i.e., the cauterization]
And this is the form of the instrument for the head purging by which oils and medicines are distilled into the nose.
And do this with silver or gold, which should be like a small open lamp, also with a small hole, and the wick similarly.
And if you want, make a closed canal like a reed, and the canal of the instrument for head purging should be flat and open; to which there is a handle on it, with which you have that when you pour into it oil or water that you want from the juices and suction causes.
Chapter 25.
Of holes in the ends of the nose.
Many times it happens that a hole occurs in the ends of the nose, and it becomes swollen and enlarged within several days if one does not observe the guard of it.
And because of this, insert that into the beginning of its appearance, and remove all that. Then place over it a cautery or burning medicine which stays in its place.
But if its eruption is delayed until it becomes swollen, then consider if it has become hard, of dusky color and with little feeling, then do not touch that with iron because it is a pustular abscess; for many times when you insert this abscess through it great harm comes to the one in whom it was.
And if the abscess is of a soft touch and not of dusky color, and it is possible to insert into it without fear, then empty out the patient and insert that without fear, and cure the place with something that corrode and constrict until it is healed.
Chapter 26.
Of suturing of the nose and lips and ears when their continuity is dissolved by ulcer or similar.
Know that when there is a solution of continuity in one of these cartilages, few perform the operation except for some men.
Therefore, whenever because of some cause, you consider that it is a fresh ulcer with its blood, then adjust the two lips of the ulcer with a suture, and then cure it until it is healed.
And if the lips of solution of continuity are already separated, and each is made healthy, then cut off each part of its visible cord until blood comes out. Then adjust the two lips with a suture and strengthen them, and over them pulverize dragon blood and sprinkle thrisat on it, and place over the powder a piece of emplaster of diafinicon, or of some other of the consolidating plasters, and let that be bound for two days or three, and then remove that plaster and change that emplaustre and let it be bound until the scabs are separated, and then cure it with ointment until it is healed.
But the way of suturing is that you add the solution of continuity or with needles as we have recommended in the suturing of the belly, or add the solution of continuity with thread just as we have taught you.
Chapter 27.
Of extraction of those causes that come in the two lips in their parts inside.
Many times in some men in the parts inside of their lips there are certain hard abscesses which resemble grit of err, and some of them are minor and some are major.
Therefore cut open the lips and split each of those hard spots throughout its parts, then fill in the wound with sage thrisat until the blood is restricted, and then wash it out with wine vinegar and cure the spot with something that constricts it until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Of incision of the flesh pressed against the gums.
Great care should be taken over the gum of flesh pressed against it which the ancients called albulus, therefore suspend that with an incision or what you have it with forceps and insert it above its root, and let the pus flow out or the blood.
Then place over it sage thrisat or other astringent or cauterizing powders.
And if that flesh returns, then insert it secondly and cauterize it, for the cautery will not return.
Chapter 29.
Of extraction of teeth with iron.
When on the surface of the teeth in the parts inside and outside and between the gums are adjusted rough scars, scabs, and some are blackened and are made citrines or green because of the gum coming through that corruption, and the teeth are bound.
Therefore cut the patient between your fingers and let him hold his head down, and scrape the teeth in which appear those scars and causes looking like sand so that no root of any cause remains, and that will be made of the blackness and of the citrineness and of the others until they are cleansed.
Therefore if it is left over what is in those of the beginning of the root, well; and if not, then you iterate the root over those on another day and again and again until the final result that you want.
And know that teeth have need of many kinds of instruments, of diverse forms and figures according to the preparation for your operation. And because the teeth that are pulled out of the gums have another shape than those that are cut between the teeth, and these shapes of instruments you should prepare right away, which you will see immediately:
Chapter 30.
Of tooth extraction.
Because you should treat the tooth of its pain with every art and delay in any extraction of it, because there is no cause that prevents its growth when it is extracted, for it is a sublimated substance inside which is an obstacle to its extraction.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, you must be certain which is the painful tooth, because many times the pain deceives the patient and it appears in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one and the pain does not cease until the sick and painful tooth is extracted, for we have seen this through the operation of the teeth.
Therefore when you are certain which is the painful tooth and know it well, then you should scarify the gum around the tooth with a spatulum in which there is some virtue, until it is dissolved from the tooth of the gum of all division, and then masticate it with your teeth, or with fine forceps little by little as you concuss it.
And then hold it firmly with strong fingers by good speech or strong stupor, and place the head of the patient between your teeth which you hold well so that he does not bite you, and then treat the tooth according to rectitude in a way that you do not break it nor give the patient greater pain.
And therefore if it is not forgotten, then take one of those instruments and place that underneath him from every part with facility and exert the removal of him as well as he can be done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is cracked or rotten, then you should cover that crack with a cloth and tighten that with the end of a fine thread so that it is not squeezed in the hour of your constriction upon that with your fingers.
And if you have any instrument which could be made of the root of a tree with the virtue of scarification, and it is necessary to extract the tooth by the head of the patient according to this way: And then open wide with scarification around the gum in every part and guard your strength that you do not break it and break a part from him, and return over the patient so that he is better than the first pain.
And if the tooth is extracted from the gum by the head of the patient and it cannot be done by the head of the patient, then it is necessary to extract it by another way which you will be certain by which it is extracted. And guard yourself that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their foolhardiness and audacity about extraction, without administering what we have recommended; for many times they bring upon men great sicknesses which are more difficult than they were squeezed, and the roots of them all wriggled, or some of those, or that you extract that with some bones of the jaw as we have testified.
And then the extraction of it is washed the mouth with warm wine or sour wine with salt.
And if blood flows from that, thus as it should, then take some quantity of vitriol and sprinkle over the wound, and if it does not work that vitriol, cauterize.
The form of the forceps and fine tweezers with which the tooth is masticated at the beginning are of long extremity, of short handle and thick, so that they are not bent when you work with them.
And be thus with handles of thick extremity so that when you stretch with those they are not bent, let them be short extremities.
And be made of iron of India descended from straight extremities, and in the extremities of those are teeth of which the one enters into the other and stretch by strong constriction, and some times the extremities of those are the form of a beak of a stork whose name is Ciconia, it is hooked, and are of strong constriction.
Chapter 31.
Of extraction of teeth and of dislocation of the bones of the jaws bitten.
When you should extract a tooth which is bitten for some cause, then you should pause over the place cut with a bandage for one or two days until the place is softened; and then insert your finger or tweezers the extremities of which are like the beak of a stork called Ciconia or hooked, the form of forceps:
The extremities of them be made inside as with file or chisel.
And if it does not obey you by these forceps or tweezers, then you should dig over the root and remove all the flesh and insert the instrument which is called atalium, it is a little lever of which the form is thus:
And be of short extremity thick a little, and not be bent so that it is not squeezed.
Thus if the cause of this is forgotten by you, it is good; and if not, aid yourself with these other instruments of which these are the forms. The form of the first instrument is triangular with a pointed end in which there is some thickness:
The shape of the second instrument is that it be triangular and of a very thin extremity.
And you are aided by this instrument having two spikes, the shape of which is to follow: and with other instruments which precede in tooth extraction; for there is made with this instrument which is assimilated to a large needle, and this is the shape:
