And I have seen a woman whose belly was pregnant with a dead fetus; and on that fire another fire was lit, and the dead child fell out. It happened to her that for a long time an ulcer developed on her navel; and when the fire opened, she began to spit up fire.
And I was called to cure him, and I treated him for a long time, but the plague did not heal.
Then I placed on him some plaster with strong attraction, and a bone came out of him, and after a few days another bone; and I was very amazed because there were bones in his belly.
And then I thought that they were the bones of the dead fetus.
Then I opened the wound and pulled out many bones; the woman was in the same condition for a long time and had a small fire coming out of her.
And I did not fear anything strange, but only that in this there is science and help to what the doctor does with his hand for curing.
Of the forms of instruments which are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of the incision by which the uterus is opened:
This is the form of the string with which the ligaments are tied, with a knot at each end of two threads. But this thread must be very thin and made of linen or cotton.
And the thickness of each rod is two fingers and the margin on both sides is one finger, and the length of each is half a palm; and in the middle of the two rods are two fastenings of that same rod, whose length is about half a palm and their thickness is about two fingers.
And these two attachments are inserted into the mouth of the uterus so that when you turn those two threads, it opens.
Other form resembling that one but thinner and longer:
It is made of linen or cotton according to the likeness of a pair of tongs. But at the end of it are two attachments like a pair of pliers; and the length of those attachments is about half a palm, and the thickness of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the uterus with those, then let the woman lie on her back, opening that which is between her thighs. And open your hand with the instrument as you do with tongs, according to how much of the mouth of the uterus the midwife wants to do what you want.
The form of incision which the ancients said:
Form of incision by which the fetus is pushed out:
Form of incision by which the large head is contained:
And it is like tongs whose ends are in the extreme, and some times it is made long like tongs, whose ends are like a pair of pliers, with which it is cut and contained. Form of incision few:
Form of an inch having a spine:
Form of an inch having two spines:
Form of spatula having two extremities by which the head is cut:
Form of two wide spatulas:
All those instruments are practiced, and when they are more refined and prepared by the artisans, then it is easier for the operator and better for men because of its quantity.
And if there are not any instruments, then you must prepare them, for the need of them is inexcusable.
Of extraction of the secundine.
When the secundine is retained after childbirth, then let the midwife retain her own milk, and then let her vomit with herbs, and strengthen your hand over the mouth and nose of it, and if through that it is expelled, good.
And if not, then take a woolen cloth and pierce it in the covering with a hole, and put into that herbs which open the mouth of the uterus, like hellebore and rue and anise and camomile and scachola arabica and cassia lignea and santal wood. All those herbs or some of them are to be soaked in water in the cloth, and then let that hang over the fire.
And then place a hollow reed in the mouth of the uterus, and hold it so that the vapor goes to the cavity of the uterus.
And then let her vomit as we have said, and the secundine will be expelled very quickly.
But if this cure is applied and the secundine is not expelled, then let the midwife soak her left hand in sesame oil or in mallow milk, and then insert that into the uterus and seek out the secundine with it, and when it has been found, pull it out little by little according to its length so that the uterus does not receive violent force. Before let it be turned right and left with ease.
And if the mouth of the uterus is constricted, we have already recommended the cure with a syringe and herbs soaked in water.
But if it is not expelled with all the causes we have recommended, then beware that you do not tire on it with too much force, but rather let what comes out of the mouth of the uterus flow to the woman's thigh, and then strengthen that with ointment and tetrafarmacon; for it will make it boil in a few days and be dissolved and expelled.
But when it boils, there is a bad smell to the head and stomach, and then let the medicines be administered according to that.
But some of the Ancients have already tried sub-fuming with hellebore and dry figs.
Form of an instrument by which the woman is sub-smoked because of the retention of menstruation and secundine:
It is made of brass or iron.
And the end be made thin in the cavity and the end wide over the fire, and the sub-fuming be done over the embers of the fire.
And hold that so that the sub-fuming is consumed and alternate it with another.
Of cure of the child's anus not perforated.
Infants who are not yet weaned and whose anuses are not perforated, for whom the tongue is very thin and swollen. And then let the midwife pierce with her finger that swelling; and if not, pierce it with a sharp spatula, and beware that it does not touch the tendon.
And then apply wool soaked in wine and oil over that area; and let that wound heal naturally.
But if you fear it has become infected, then insert a cannula into the wound for several days and leave it in place until the patient feels the urge to urinate.
And it sometimes happens that the anus becomes obstructed by a blood clot or abscess; then when this obstruction has been removed, let it be treated with the medicine and lead plaster as we have said above.
Regarding the treatment of fistulas which develop subsequently:
Fistulas which occur near the anus and produce pus and a thick discharge are found near the anus or in the space between the buttocks.
There is either one fistula or several.
And when this nodule becomes old and discharges thick, white or yellow pus, sometimes these fistulas penetrate deep into the body.
And those that penetrate are known by what comes out of them through the exit and by their flatulence; and sometimes the patient suffers from an exit, and often worms come out of them.
And sometimes these fistulas penetrate into the bladder and into the vagina; and sometimes they penetrate into the joint of the thigh and into the knee.
And the way to know whether a fistula has penetrated into the anus or not is to put your finger into the anus and try to gently push a needle or piece of iron into the fistula when there is no pain.
But if it hurts when you do this, then gently push in a needle or a piece of lead or horsehair until you feel the needle or hair against your finger.
And if you don't feel anything at all and there is no discharge of pus or worms or flatulence as we have said, then know that it has not penetrated, and then proceed with the treatment.
And when the fistula has penetrated into the bladder or vagina, then the sign of this is the discharge of urine from it and the prohibition of this from that, so that the wound is allowed to heal.
But if it has penetrated into the joint of the thigh or into the bone and causes pain in that area, then the sign is that when you push a needle into it, there is no pain in the bone, but pain in the circuit of the joint; and that pus comes out of it occasionally; and that this is not helped by medicine nor healed by ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas, there is no cure at all, and their treatment is a laborious and vain task for foolish doctors who talk about it.
But in the one that is not penetrating and not ancient, healing with iron is expected according to what I have told you and what has already happened.
And it is that the patient should lie on his back with his head elevated and his legs raised up against him, with his thighs bent towards his stomach, and then gently push a needle or piece of iron into the fistula until you feel where the needle goes in.
And if he feels pain around his neck, then gently put your finger into his neck.
And if you feel the needle going in by itself without pain, or you feel something soft under your finger, then know for certain that it is penetrating; so do not work on this, because there is no healing in this as we have said.
But some say that it is healed in some people with vinegar.
And the medicine by which this is expected to heal quickly is that you apply a gentle cautery according to the size of the fistula, as it was preceded, and insert that hot iron into the fistula until it comes out around the neck; then repeat this two or three times until you know for sure that all those flesh-colored, fistula-like tissues which resemble a feather cane have been dried up.
Then cure that with water wrapped in wool until all those dried-up tissues are healed, and then cure it with medicines that generate and consolidate flesh, and if it is healed, well; but if not, then it will never be healed by any other treatment.
And if the needle goes in and doesn't penetrate your finger which is in the anus, and between that and the needle there is a hard, flesh-like substance or swelling, and you see the fistula where it breaks through the surface of the skin, then dry up the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you will see that it dries up as the needle goes in and out at the place where the tip of the needle comes out; and so the flesh is freed and cut.
Then cleanse those flesh-like tissues which are similar to a goose feather cane, from which nothing comes out.
But if blood flows out of it when you press on those tissues, then the millstone that is and the pus that comes out should be cauterized with fire or sharp medicine, for cautery with fire dries up those flesh-like tissues and stops the flow of blood by drying up the humors.
Then cure that with water wrapped in wool or in clean linen until the spot turns black, and then cure the wound with a hard ointment that generates and consolidates tough flesh.
And so are medicines which have astringency and drying power; when it is restored, it is healed and you need not fear relapse.
But if the fistula has already broken through into the perineum and is prolonged from there across the surface of the body, then put your finger in the anus and examine it.
And so if you feel the needle and between you and it there is a hard substance or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then it is not an operation on it but according to the way of trust and hope.
This can be done in one of three ways: by cauterizing it as we have said; by drying it up so that you can make the opening in the anus smaller and the cure more effective; or by not cutting into the rectum so that you do not pierce the membrane containing the rectum, but let the patient go about his business naturally; then cure him with what we have said for the wound will surely heal quickly; or by prolonging the swelling and draining out the pus which has accumulated under the skin as we have said, or with a needle or another sharp instrument, then cleanse those flesh-like tissues which are in the incision near where you can pierce it with pressure; then force open the wound so that the entire wound is healed, and if the fistula which breaks through into the anus has not been consolidated, and the pus drains out of the anus, and the patient suffers from a discharge, let him be cured with what we have said. And sometimes the fistula ends like this: when you insert the needle into the fistula, it is on the side of the anus around the surface of the body with the skin and at the margin of the anus, then take a needle of piercing extremity which is prepared in the shape of a needle as follows:
and insert it into that with its wrapper or around it; and then insert the needle into the fistula until you reach the opening in him.
But if it has penetrated into the margin of the anus near inside, then pull out the needle from that hole as you put your finger in the anus; and when you have pulled out the end of the needle, press both ends together, and tie them together for a day or two, and when you have also inserted the needle into the flesh, press it until those flesh-like tissues are pressed in between the two ends of the needle which are tied together.
And then treat the wound until it is healed and closed.
But if the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate in any way you have prepared, unless there is motion in the wound, for it is not up to you to do so as not to injure the skin; and then treat it until it is healed.
The shape of the spathe is spiny where the fistula is split: its curvature is very acute and the other side is not acute, so as not to injure anything that is not necessary, as well as the skin.
On the stitching of hemorrhoids in which blood runs in the incision of those.
Hemorrhoids are of two types, for some are between the anus like charcoal, or others that are like fat with hair. And those are small and large, and the blood runs from them constantly and is one, and some are inside, and outside the anus, and at its ends.
But those that are outside the anus are according to the months of little humidity, of which yellow water or a little blood runs with a continuous body, and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is inside the anus is that you command the patient to sit and be compressed until the anus dries and the hemorrhoids appear to you. Then you cut and suspend them with your nails or what you have those by your fingernail, and then cut those according to their roots.
But if the nails are not held in those by their moisture and softening of them, then take those with rough cloth or a stick or woolen thread, and extract those with your fingers, and then cut those and powder some powders over them near the incision so that they are like cautery; or cauterize those according to what is preceded in the chapter on cauterization. And then treat those with the other treatment until they are healed.
And if the anus does not obey defecation, then anoint the patient with ointment in which there is a little biting, and let him be washed with that water that is in the anus and let him be compressed gently when he excretes.
But if the hemorrhoids are outside the anus, then the cause of them is slight, and it is that you take those with your nail or that you take them with a stick and cut those, and then treat those as we have said until they are healed.
And whoever does the incision, keep in mind that you administer suturing of them according to this way: take wax wrapped and insert that into a needle and then extract the hemorrhoids at their tops, and make the needle penetrate with the other part of the wax into the root of the other part, and wrap both ends of the wax in the part lower than the needle and tightly; and narrow the hemorrhoid with a strong compression, and tie off the wax and extract the needle.
And do that to all, and leave one and not sew it, so that the excess of blood runs from it.
And then place over the anus cloth soaked in rose oil or cerate; and place that on the anus and command the patient to rest. And then leave it until it scabs; and when it is healed, treat those with ointment or other treatment until they are healed.
And if they are old and not healed with that medicine, then you should cut those with the point of a spathe or your nail until they become moist and let the scabs be above, which prevents them from healing; and then treat those until they are healed.
Then you wound them more violently than the first time so that blood flows and they become excoriated; and then treat those until they are healed.
Know that an ulcer, it is a key, and it is not unless there is a round spot according to the color of the body resembling a head of a nail, and it occurs all over the body and mainly in the parts below the feet and on the fingers, and from there you should have pain.
And then let it be cut what is in the circuit of the nail, and let it be held with forceps or a stick, and let it be uprooted from its root; and then treat that.
And if you want, cauterize the second as is preceded in its own chapter.
But a wart is a small, rough, round, fistulated eminence on the surface of the body, and many times it comes in the hands of children.
And its treatment is that you stretch and cut it, or narrow it with wax or hair so that it falls off. And if you want, cauterize it as we have said, with fire or acute medicine.
But a mole is a small, felted or fistulated eminence on the surface of the body, which occurs around the anus, and when it is rubbed, it is centered in it like a bite of an ant, and it is made all over the body.
And its treatment is that you take a reed of peacock feather or of bird of prey, and let it be strong, and place it on the mole so that it contains the circle of the reed from the parts around it; and then turn your hand with the reed so that you cut what is in the circuit of the mole, and let him narrow the mole at its root.
And if you want, do it with an eraser or iron according to this form:
Let the part above the reed be drawn out to thinness, soft (so that it is not perforated), wrapped so that over the finger the appearance of it is abraded and the revolution of it.
And if you want to cauterize the mole according to what is said before in the cauterization of atoms in the chapter on cautery.
Of the form of the instruments that are administered to give cautery.
Some are made of silver or brass or melted lead or hammered, and some are made from those instruments small and large according to the ones that are administered, for that which is administered in the treatment of small children is small; and of those whose anuses are sore or painful, their cauteries are small.
The form of a large and few cautery:
The tip of the reed above in which the pouch is tied be wide according to this way; and let the distinction where the pouch is tied as well, thus many times.
And the tip below it, which is inserted into the anus, be soft  (so that it is not open), drawing out to thinness; and in one side of it there are two slits, and in the other side a slit, and let the width of the slit according to the thickness of the veins and more thick a little.
Let there be a bag in which are placed the medicines of animal bladder or sheep's wool, and its size should be a palm.
And let it be pierced with small holes as it is rolled up; and if the space between the threads, make it the thickness of the said finger.
And then insert into those threads strong gall of raw gall which is of the galls; and apply with that bag to it; and when the bag is applied to it, let it be tightly bound at the top of the chest between two fingers with a strong constriction, and then cauterize each thread with it: But the quality of cauterizing with it is that you fill the bag with medicine, which should be warmed according to the amount of the patient's tolerance; and beware that it is not too hot nor too cold.
And then the patient should be reclined on his back, and his legs raised up and place under them a board on which there are men or a plank; and then anoint the end of the cautery with oil or egg white or mucilage of fenugreek or similar; and then insert that into the anus with ease, and the minister should squeeze the bag and press with two fingers and empty with force into the intestine everything that is in the chest; and then pull out the cautery and let the patient raise his legs to the wall.
And if the medicine forces that to come out, let it as much as possible; and if it sleeps with that for a whole night, it is better for healing.
But if you cauterize that in the illnesses of a swollen belly and its output is bloody, then repeat the cautery until it is healed.
And if in the anus there are hemorrhoids or painful sores, then let the cautery be very subtle and thin, of those cauteries with which children are cauterized.
Of treatment of sores:
You have already said in the division of the treatment of sores what was convenient for the book, and I say thus the treatment of those with hand and medicines according to their kind.
Therefore I say that sores are varied according to that by which the sores were made and according to the place on which they occurred.
And the causes for which many times sores are made are thus with stone, or incision of a sword or dagger, or blow from a spear, or staff, or arrow, or similar to those of many causes.
But the sores that are in parts of the body are thus with sore which falls on the head, or neck, or feet, or belly, or thigh, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I have recounted the treatment of some sores so that you pause on that reasoning and rule for other sores.
And I will begin with simple sores of the head, because the recollection of the composite ones will come to the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore I say that when it happens in the head a simple sore and there is no fracture, then consider: if it is by blow of stone or similar, and the nerve has only slightly been struck and it is a large wound and you fear on that patient development of an ulcer, cauterize and flebotomize the cephalic vein immediately, and do not delay that; and draw from that blood according to the amount of the strength of him if there is no other prohibiting cause of phlebotomy, especially if the blood which comes out of the wound is little.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side from the wounded part; and keep the patient from repletion and from wine and heavy foods.
But if any cause of diminishment prohibits you, then let it be diminished in food and not come near to meat nor wine.
And apply over the wound, if it happens on him ulceration, cut garlic infusing it in rose oil or wine in which it is made stiff.
But if he is certain of ulceration, then apply over the wound, if it is fresh with its blood and the air has not changed that powder which I will soon recount below.
And if the air has already changed that, then apply some ointment until it is healed.
But if the wound is large or it is from incision of sword or similar, and its lips are not brought together by pus, then bring those together with suture according to that which I will recount about their suture.
But if the wound is already uncovered of bone and pen, and there is no retention in it except for a little suspension, then insert into that and treat the wound with medicines and restore the flesh by applying on the nerve, and command the patient to abstain from foods in which there is heaviness: garlic and leeks and similar to them, until he is healed.
And if you see in the nerve putrefaction and it does not adhere to the bone, then insert all that rotten thing and cut it out, and treat that.
But if in these sores there is an artery or vein from which blood might flow and it has not been stopped by medicines, then press on the artery; and if you find that not-incised, then insert into it with spatum or ligature; and if necessity compels you that what we have said does not suffice, then cauterize until the blood is stopped.
And if these sores are simple and small, then the cause of them is a wound; therefore you powder over those this powder, because it solidifies that before the air changes those.
And the manner of the powder is this: take oil of unripe dates one part, blood of dragon tree two parts, gum not-extinguished three parts; let all be pounded and sifted, and of that let the wound be filled up, and let it be bound with good constriction so that it adheres to that, and let the blood coagulate.
And then bind that above with powders and let it be licked by the tongue, and do not loosen it while it is thus until it is ulcerated, because the medicine does not work for it to be healed.
And sometimes you make that alone with cautery when there is no dragon tree at hand or its blood.
And you make this operation on large sores when it seems that it should be applied to them and they are bound up, and they are recent wounds with their blood which the air has not changed.
And if the air has changed them, and especially when it is in summer time, then there is no need for the position of this powder, but then treat those with what we have said, therefore you apply over those some ointments that are digested, or you apply over those poultices made of flour of the seed of wheat with water and honey until they are soft; and then treat those with the other treatment until they are healed.
But if it happens with a wound of fracture in bone, and it is small, then you draw that out with a probe.
And I will say the treatment for fractures of the head where it is pierced.
Of sores in the neck:
But if the wound happens in the neck, then there is no difference in treatment, and it is between the wounds of the head when it is simple.
But if the nerves of the neck are cut or an artery, then if it is nerve let it not be bound tightly with anything that makes vehement strictness, as with cinchona and cinchona-tree which is verjuice and similar to them because they hurt the nerves and contract that. And do not put any pressure on the plague at all, because the substance of the nerves is fragile and its continuation goes to the brain, which is the source of all the members.
And if it is healed by light medicines, such as when the wound is washed with warm water, or when there is an infusion of red or white oil, and when it is washed with warm water and the other stones are applied according to this manner, then let it be moistened.
But if the plague is large, then administer a suture or a bandage, and let it be cured with powders until it is healed.
But if there is a depth or an abscess in the lowest part of the pus, then pierce it in the most depressed location.
But if an artery is cut in the plague and bleeding ensues, then ligate or coagulate it, or cauterize it if need demands it.
But if the vertebrae of the neck or all of it is cut, and the veins of the throat are spared, then you adjust the wound of the neck with a suture over the cane of the neck and do not touch it; before doing this, equalize that and reduce it to its natural figure, and then tightly bind it with a decent bandage; let it be treated with ointments in which there is stiffness and excitation until it is healed.
But if the bone has been fractured, then pierce it and extract all of the fragments that you can; let what is not apparent heal within, and separate it and the efflux from it be alleviated.
And if the plague is recent, then powder it with a powder for which you have been prescribed. But if a fistula is formed in the plague, then cure it with that which its recollection will come to you in due time.
If it is a blow from a lance or spear, and you see the depth of it, then consider that if there is windiness from the wound, then know that the plague is mortal.
And if there is no depth to it and the plague is recent with its blood, then you put on a powder for the first hour and do not bind it so that the blood is not retained in the depth of it and that it returns to the heart and the patient is saved; instead, put on an attractive ointment twice a day.
And if the ointment is not available to you, then put on the orifice of the plague a piece of wool so that it sucks up what there is of humidity; and let the patient sleep on the plague so that what is in it flows out.
But if three days or more have passed and no spasms or tremors of the heart nor mal-stricture occur to him, and you see the other dispositions as good, then know that the plague is saved; then cure it with cauterization and with the other cure until it is healed.
But if its healing is delayed and the wound stays open for a long time, then know that a fistula has been done; cure it therefore according to its chapter.
And if the plague is done by a spear or lance and is simple on the surface of the foot or leg, then cure it with that which is preceded by a suture if it is large, or powder if it is small.
But if the bone has been fractured and there are only a few fragments in it, then you twist the wound and cut to extract those fragments, if they are separated. But if they are not separated, then let them be until the plague is healed, for their extraction is alleviated.
And of the other plagues of accidents in the other members the cure is the same as that which we have said before.
Of plagues of the belly:
The disposition by which it happens in the belly is either great or small or middling.
And a small disruption is done difficultly in another way, and you see that the reduction of them is more difficult if you do not cut off the intrusion of them to their place at once, for it opens and is swollen because the insertion of it is made difficult.
And therefore the best disruption is middling, for in it the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is difficult in both species mentioned before.
And know that the suture of the belly is according to four manners, but two sutures are common which are used in sutures of the other plagues of the body.
And two other sutures are proper to the other plagues, but indeed more proper in the suture of the belly.
And I will count for you each one with exposition and declaration.
Therefore I say that when the plague is small and some cause of the intestine comes out through it, and it is made difficult to reduce it, then it is in one of two manners: or by smallness of the disruption as we have said, or when there is inflation of the intestine by the coldness of the air.
And therefore when that is so, then you must warm it so that you soak a sponge or cloth in tepid water only by itself, or boil in squinant water, and cyperum, and spic, and embrocations with it the intestine until the inflammation is resolved.
And sometimes it does this in the thing which has been made stiff, and it is better than water only in resolution of the inflammation.
And therefore when you see the inflammation resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which is mixed honey and mallows, for it eases the insertion of it with a little cause.
But if the insertion of it is excused by this cure, then split the disruption a little with an instrument by which the hemorrhoids are split.
And the primary cure is better when it is proper and not dry, and there is no occupation to dry near necessity and defalimentation.
Of the instrument, know its form:
One part of it is crooked acute, and the other part not-acute, and its extremity subtle, and may not be with the subtlety of a spatula, but rather obtuse a little.
And therefore when the plague is enlarged and goes out of the intestine, then you must make its reduction according to the form of it natural, in its proper place, if it is possible, for it is better.
But when the disruption is ample and it is in the lower part of the belly, then let the patient lie on his back, and put his feet above; and if it is in the upper part, then let him lie on his stomach and raise his head and feet.
And likewise if the disruption is in one of the two parts of the belly, then pay your attention and your consideration equally on both sides so that the part in which there is the plague be higher than the other.
And thus you must administer this in great plagues and middling plagues.
And in small wounds, apply what you have prepared, and ensure that the surgeon is between your thighs, holding the wound with his hand and closing its lips, and uncover from one to another any cause of the wound, or other.
And this is the common manner of suturing: you take the needle, or count of needles according to the quantity of the amplitude of the wound; and leave from the extremity of the disruption of the ring finger, and tighten the needle one, other that you put thread in both ends of the cord, with both ends of the thong which is below the cord of the parts within, so that you make it penetrate from that part; and you have already tied both ends of the cord and two ends of the thong, and there are four orders.
And then tighten with double thread around the needle, and some times from two parts together, and then tie both lips and the wound by decent tying; and leave the thickness of the finger, and take another needle in that, and tie that with thread as is tied with the first needle; and do not cease to do this if it is necessary with needles until you have completed all the contraction of the wound.
And the needles should be between thickness and thinness, because thin needles easily pierce the flesh; and thick ones are difficult to enter into the cord, and therefore that they may be easier in subtlety and thickness.
And it should not be that the needle is tied in two ends of the cord near to each other, because the flesh would be pierced easily and the wound would open before its healing.
And they should not be prolonged so that the wound is not prohibited from healing.
And then pierce the ends of the needles so that they do not hurt the patient while sleeping; and you shall put over the sutures powdered herbs spread from all parts, which have the ends of the needles; and leave them until you know that the wound is healed.
And these species of suturing with needles in whatever manner are more convenient for small wounds, because they have in their suturing one needle or two or around them.
But the second suturing is common: and it is that you administer suturing with the four ends, of the cord and two ends of the thong, in one go, with a needle in which thread is wrapped equal in thinness and thickness.
And then when with the needle you have penetrated these four ends, reduce the needle from that meting part from which you started, so that the thread drawn box of the uppermost part to the wound, so that the suturing is according to suturing of cloth with which the cloths are tied tightly.
And put between each suturing the quantity of the thickness of the little finger.
And this suturing is known to men.
And with such suturing I healed the wound which was inflicted on a man in his belly, and it was wounded with a dagger, and the rupture of the wound was more than a palm, and it had come out from the intestines around two palms from the intestine of the middle place, and it was the disruption in the middle of the member; and then I reduced it after the intestine had been pierced by the wound for twenty-four hours.
And the wound was healed in the space of fifteen days or around, and he recovered and is well; and then he lived for many years, and is in his dispositions.
And other doctors had judged that he would not be healed; and by miracle it was healed, because I did not cure him with ointment, but put on the wound cut wine twice a day and frequent washing with honey water until he was healed.
But I will say two kinds of suturing proper according to the account of the Word.
And it is that you start with suturing of the cord, and interlace needle from the outside part to the inside part.
And so when the needle penetrates in the cord and in that part within according to straightness and in length of all the body, leave the end of the thong in which you have interlaced the needle; and make the needle penetrate in the other end from the inside part to the outside in the other end of the finger.
And when you have done that penetration, then do it another time in that meting end of the finger from the outside part to the inside; and leave the end of the thong which is in this side, and make the needle penetrate in the other end of the inside part to the outside.
And make the needle penetrate with which you are making it penetrate in the thong in the end of the finger which is in the part of him so that all that penetrates.
And then start from that meting side and sew it with the end which is of the thong in the other side, and pull out the needle of the cord which penetrates, and then reduce it to that cord and sew the end of the thong which is in the other side with this end of the finger; and pull out it of the cord which is on the part of him.
And do that once and another time, and do it one time after the other so that you sew all that is outside according to that example.
And the sum of this manner of suturing is that you sew with suturing of hides with which hide is sewn, so that you sew the thong one time from the end of the cord, and leave it another time so that you have completed it.
But the second proper species which I say is the command of him and for him recomposed, is that you sew it according to semilitude with which all medicines are sewn, so that the ends of him are joined to each other in convenient nature; and apply the end of another of him and the end of the finger to the other end of him, and that is done according as I have recomposed it.
Because you put the needle in the end of the finger at your side from the outside part, and make it penetrate to the inside in that sole, and leave two ends of the thong; and then turn the needle and make it penetrate from the outside to the inside in two ends of the thong.
And then turn those ones, and make some of the inside part penetrate to the outside in two ends of the finger which are in the contrary side.
And this manner of suturing is better than common suturing, and it is suturing of which prior memory precedes.
This is the command of Gualia
And then they said some one, the suturing of the belly is straightened with two needles, so that you interlace in these ones a thread, and start with penetration of needle from what is on you, and make it penetrate within to the other side according to suturing of those whom hide is sewn equally.
And know that when rupture is in the middle place of the belly, then the suturing of it is more difficult than in other places of the belly.
But the judgment of medicine of wound is judgment of other wounds.
The which cause is that when you know the wound recent with its blood before that air alters it, and reduce the intestine and sew it and adapt it decently, then put over it powdery incarnative.
And if the air has already altered the wound, then apply some alum to that area so that it makes a hard stone and traps the bile, and let it solidify and heal; and treat it as you would any other wound until it is healed.
But if you do not have the medicines with you, then apply it immediately as soon as the wound starts to discharge pus, and change the poultice twice a day, morning and evening, as I have taught you, until it is healed, for you need no other treatment in various conditions when wounds are simple.
But if you fear that the wound might spread to the main limbs, then soak lamb's wool in warm oil of good quality, or rose oil, and apply it around the areas between the angles and below the ankles.
And if he feels pain or putrefaction in his intestines, where many times it happens, then anoint that with black styptic vinegar, especially if the putrefaction is in the intestine and there is an open wound inside its cavity.
And know that a wound in the large intestine has a slower healing process than one in the small intestines.
And it is difficult to heal because the intestine called "gut" does not receive healing from a wound that occurs in it at all; and this is due to the great multitude of veins that are in it, and because of its size compared to those, and because of the subtlety of that organ, and because of its proximity to nerve nature.
But if what proceeds from the wound is the intestine, and you manage to get it out, return that according to your skill by which you reduce the intestines with equal measure.
But if it has passed into space and is already green or black, then that you strengthen that with bile on the spot where it has turned black, so that a flow of blood comes out, for there are veins and arteries in the tissue.

Of the healing of ulcer and fistula.

Of the treatment of ulcers and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an ulcer occurs in the fleshy parts and the space of the ulcer is prolonged so that it makes a collection of pus, and then it is broken and perforated, and the pus is discharged from it, and the site becomes hollow as if it were a vessel, and the cord which is above it is like a veil with a thin cloth, and there has been no subsequent corruption, and it does not impress upon the bone or nerve or ligament, it is called an ulcer, and is not named a fistula.
But if the space is prolonged so that corruption occurs in some member, then it is called a fistula and not an ulcer.
And the treatment of it is that you cut off the whole cord as it is turned over and most especially when it is thinned out and made with a cloth, and bandages which do not adhere to the site by its corruption.
But if you expect the cord to adhere and that corruption has not been accomplished by subsequent consequence, and there is passage in the cord of pus, then you should treat it with an enema.
And consider the ulcer, and if it is great and the arm which you see gives off a fetid odor, then enema that with an Egyptian medicine which is of oil and sharp wine and linseed received in parts equalized in a vessel, and let them be heated over fire until the medicine becomes thick as honey.
And then take from it that which is necessary and dissolve it with water and honey; and enema with it the ulcer, and tighten the orifice of it, and let the medicine remain in it for the quantity of two hours.
And then remove that with discretion; and do this for so many days until the ulcer is cleansed and the pain abated.
And in some cases it is enema with water of ashes when that medicine is not available to you; and take the ashes of wood and put on it water, and then boil that and strain it, and enema that with it until you are certain that the ulcer has been relieved.
And if the patient has no tolerance for the Egyptian medicine or water of ashes, then enema that with water and honey mixed with linseed pounded into powder, and enema that with wine and honey mixed because of the nature of honey is to cleanse and wash, and wine makes the ulcer adhere especially when in the wine there is surplus of astringency and dryness.
And when you have done this several times and you are certain that the ulcer has abated, then enema that with that which makes the flesh grow on it.
And take the ointment of daphnoides and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine, or enema that with some other medicines and unguents which I have written about in the treatise on unguents.
And if the orifice of the ulcer is narrow so that the cannula of the enema cannot be received, then you should enlarge it with a small iron little by little, and put on it cotton wrapped in Egyptian ointment or in balsamic ointment until it is enlarged.
And likewise if the orifice is wide, then you should close it with a stitch and put on it quantity in which between the enema is added.
And if the orifice from which the arm hangs is elevated to the parts above, then dry that in the pus base of it so that the arm does not run into it below because when the arm is constricted in the depth of the ulcer the flesh engendered in it.
And if it is not possible for you to dry the ulcer in the surrounding of the bone of pus base according as you wish, then be assured that you prepare the member with preparation so that from it the arm runs longer according as it appears to you and that there is no cause for the arm in it.
But that which is on the orifice of the ulcer, which abates in every hour because it draws what is constricted in it of the arm. And so that you know whether what runs from it of the arm is pus or little, or is digested, or is undigested.
And you consider the site of that ulcer if in it the patient feels pain or not, and if there is pus in it.
And when your medication is prolonged in this manner, and you see the matter that has exuded through the orifices of the sinus already adhered, or with its own adhesion, then on the third day, loosen it with a poultice, and when you loosen the plague, then change the dressing with another one and renew the ointment.
But if from the sinus there exudes a subtle humor, then do not despair of its adhesion, because its healing is proper, for there are some men in whom the generation of flesh occurs in their plagues, and of those in whom the contrary occurs as well.
And if a bad indigestion has exited from the sinus for several days, then know that the sinus is not adhered; and if its adhesion is delayed and its cause prolonged, then you should reduce the quantity of medicines, and if the medicines are wet in their substance and in their efficacy, they are dried as well as a defying poultice when there is excess addition of zegi.
And the most posterior one that is treated is to take a defying poultice and dissolve it in rose oil, and over it apply honey wine equal in its substance, and then comfort him with that and use it.
And already make a demonstration in that action when there is no humor, by taking a defying poultice and honey when it is cut into that it becomes thickened, and use it; or take myrrh and aloe and mix them together, and grind everything and pulverize it over the honey as it is over the fire, and then line it over the fabric and press that over the sinus; and line with the honey which you have cut into so that it becomes thickened, and pulverize it over the fabric and press that.
And sometimes they are administered with it a long aristology and roots of the heavenly lily (and that is the heavenly lily) and farina of err and centaurea, and use those simples or adjust them, grind them and sift them and pulverize them over the honey I have recommended; and use it.
And if the sinus is prolonged and not healed by this cure, then know that the corruption is in its preon, and already make inquiry into the bones or other hard members or into the ligaments; then you should cure it with a fistular cure as has been said above.
Of the cure of panaricus and nail abrasion and digit addition incision and section.
Panaricus is an apostema that occurs under the nail of the toe of the hand or foot, and seldom under the other toes, and as it is prolonged and its cure is less valued, it becomes apostematized from cauterization, or it becomes corrupted, and it exudes humor within that corrodes the roots of the nail, and it corrupts all of that, and its corruption very much pervades the bone within which has a fetid odor, and its extremity becomes lumpy, and its color becomes dusky.
And therefore when you have that from which we have spoken in the division and do not comfort your cure, then know that you must cut away with iron all of the excess that is rotted from the nail and then cauterize the wound accordingly, for the cautery is beneficial in this.
But if the bone is sound and the nail is healthy, and the additions of the nail are pushing inwards on the flesh between the parts of the nails, and it begins to sting and irritate that, then know that you should pause a fine needle beneath the angle of the nail which is pricking the flesh, and dislodge that upwards and insert that flesh with ease, and apply upon that which rises from the flesh medicaments that burn and corrode within so that everything is cleaned; and then cure it with ointments once it is healed.
But if the corruption is already within the bone, then know that you must cut out the bone and extract it, for it will not be healed as long as the rotten bone remains in it.
And if the corruption and rot extend into the finger, then you should insert it into one of the knuckles according to which is preselected by its memory, and then cure that once it is healed.
But if there is percussion or abrasion upon the nail and subsequently violent pain in that, then know that you must at the beginning bleed the patient, and then dry the nail with a pointed spatula with a transversal section above it, and beware that the section does not pervade into the flesh that is beneath the nail, for you will cause violent pain to the patient, and its cause will be the generation of excess flesh in the place; and then cure that place with what relieves the pain.
But a finger that is born in the hands of some men is very seldom all flesh, and rarely within it bones, or rarely within it nails.
And some of it are born from the root of the joint, or its generation is from some knuckles of the fingers. But that which is born in some knuckles of the finger does not become sick, and that which is born from the joints is very much sickened.
And of those which are born fleshy, its section is easier, and you should insert that behind its roots with a broad spatula.
But of that which is born from the root of the joint, its cure is difficult; and therefore beware of its incision.
But in that which is born in a finger from the knuckles, know that you must at the beginning insert the flesh of it with a round incision into the bone, and then cut with one of those convenient saws for that, and then cure it once it is healed.
But consolidation which occurs in the fingers between their motions many times or from generation of the person who has it, or from consolidation of plague, or from burn of fire, or from similar things, therefore know that you should dry that consolidation so that the fingers return to their natural form.
And then place between their bands of them soaks in rose oil so that they are not consolidated too easily, and separate those or place between them a thin sheet of lead within which is consolidated according to what it should be.
And similarly if consolidation occurs in some fingers with the palm, then you should dry that consolidation according to how it is prepared and convenient for the form of the member.
Of incision of veins, these are varicose.
Varicose are veins, and they are grossly rolled vessels full of melancholy humors which flow in the motions of the members of the body, and their greatest occurrence is in the legs, and mostly in the legs of travelers and laborers and carriers.
Therefore know that you should administer purgation of the body from black cholera several times with strong expulsion, and then bleed the patient from the basilic vein.
But their cure by iron is according to two methods: one is that it be cut and that the black blood be drawn out, and the other method is that the vein be raised up and all of it drawn out.
Its incision is according to this method: at the beginning moisten the leg with warm water so that the gross blood is dissolved which is feculent of the leg, and then press the leg from above on the part pus uppermost of the thigh into the part below the groin with a bandage, and then dry the vein in one place, or two, or three with broad incision, and then raise up the black blood with the sole of the hand from the part below the leg within upwards so that of the blood is expelled the quantity which you desire and which the strength of the patient will bear.
And then lead them away, and command that they abstain from foods engendering black bile.
And repeat the treatment by phlebotomy when the veins are full and this is noticed in the patient.
But the elevation of them is according to this manner: you raise the leg of the patient if in it there are veins, and then put him into a bath and rub his leg with warm water until it reddens and the veins are dilated; or he be exercised by vigorous exercise, if a bath is not available, so that the limb is heated.
And then cut the tendon in the direction of the vein either along its length or behind him below the pain, or at the very bottom of him near the ankle.
And then open the tendon with nails and gouge out the vein in every part where it appears to the sense, and one must observe the appearance of it that turns black at times.
And so when you have evacuated and from the tendon you see it white like a cord.
And then put that under the leg so that it is elevated and is supported by the tendon, and suspend it with a silk thread from an iron hook.
And then cut another section just above that section by three fingers, and gouge out the tendon from the part above the vein where it appears.
And then elevate that and insert it into the posterior section below the ankle, and then extract it from the second section above it, and then extract it from the section above it.
And do this until you extract it from the section that is most superior or highest of all the sections, so that all is evacuated; and then insert it.
And if he does not obey you and does not allow himself to be extracted and elevated, then insert a strong bent needle into him and ligature it, or put under him the edge of your hand and move your hand around to every part where there is an abundance. And beware that it is not incised; for if it is cut then it has become difficult for you to elevate the part and it has come upon the patient through that injury.
And when all of it has been evacuated out, then place on the site of the wound wool soaked in wine and rose oil, and cure it until it is healed.
And if the vein is contracted having tortuosity in which there is an envelope of its parts, and it is not according to rectitude as we have said, then you must split that at every part of its tortuosity and the sites of its envelope, and then suspend it with nails so that you elevate all of it.
And beware that you do not incision the vein and that you do not harm it, for it will be difficult for you to elevate it; and exercise your prudence in that.
Form of the incision with which the vein is dried:
It is not made of iron nor is it of acute extremity so that it does not harm the vein; and it has great flexibility of the thread, for if it were subtle it would cut the vein through its subtlety; there is in it superfluous thickness just as we have said.
Of extraction of the leg vein.
This vein is engendered in the legs in hot lands just like Al-Ateji in the land of the Arabs and in hot and arid lands of little tenderness, and in which there is no herb or grass.
And it is engendered strongly in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And its gestation is from putrefaction that comes beneath the tendon just as venomous snakes and worms and insects and worms engendered between the tendon and flesh.
And sign of the occurrence of its coming is that vein which inflames violently in the leg, and then the site becomes blistered; and then the vein begins to emerge from the site of that blister as if cut from a plant.
And then when you see the end of it coming out, you must wrap around it a piece of lead weighing two drachmas, and hang it suspended from the leg.
And as many times as you see it go forth for some cause to its parts, wrap that around it in lead and suspend it. And if the vein is prolonged, then you insert a part of it and wrap around the remainder; and do not incision that from cut so that it is drawn out, for if you incision it it is contracted and goes into the flesh and causes an abscess and swelling in the site and bad ulceration.
Because it must be drawn out slowly and extracted little by little so that it is evacuated from its end and does not draw back into the body for any cause.
And from this vein it emerges in some men who are in its length of five or ten palms, and I have seen one emerge twenty-palms.

Of extraction of arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to their species, and according to the parts of the body in which they are found, for their diversity is that some have great points, and small, and concave, and blunt, some are solid; and of them there are those that have three angles, and of those that have tongues, and of those that are stars.
And the signs of the soul are when an arrow pierces it, and breaks the bone, and is lodged in the cyphac which is above the brain, for then occurs violent soddening, and scotomia, and vertigo, and heat in the holes, and inflammation and heat of the tongue, and spasm and mixture of understanding, and vomiting of bile, and strong pain is abscinded, and the word is destroyed, and the voice broken, and moisture white like powder exits from that spot, and the eyes are like water.
And therefore if these signs appear to you, then refrain from curing and extracting the arrow if it is not outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it pierces the heart are that it is near the left nipple, and that its point is lodged in a hard cause, and it is not a hollow cause; and very quickly the movement of the arrow is like the movement of the pulse, and flows from the wound black blood, and causes chilling of the extremities, and sweating chill, and fainting; and therefore know that death descends with certainty.
And the signs when the arrow has wounded the lung are spuming of blood from the wound, and the vessels that are near the throat are swollen, and the color of the patient is changed, and he exhales great spirit, and demands drawing in of air quickly.
And if the arrow pierces the membrane which is in the feet, then the pain will be near the little ribs, and it is made great by violent pain and by sighing, and all the limbs of the muscles are not weakened.
And if the arrow pierces the stomach, then quickly you will see from the wound some undigested food.
And if the arrow pierces the belly and is lodged in it, then you will see some opening of the wound or the membrane or intestine which is broken, and there is no hope for curing it by extraction of the arrow.
And if the arrow pierces the bladder and exits urine outside, and processes from it some cause to the parts outside, and very quickly there is pain upon the patient, then know that it is mortal.
But the other limbs such as the face, the neck, and the throat, and the shoulders and the helper, and the spine of the back, and the breastbone, and the groin, and the hip, and their likes of the limbs, are saved according to the greatest dispositions when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery, and is not a poisonous arrow.

The veins from which phlebotomy is usually performed are .xxx., of which there are in the head .xvi., there are two pulsating veins which appear close to the ears, some call them alhasisain; two veins that appear on both temples; and two veins that are in the two tear ducts of the eyes that are called aspicientz; and a vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein in the tip of the nose; and two guidegi that are on the neck; and two veins that are in the upper lip; and two veins that are in the lower lip; and — these are the veins that are called algelic —; and there are two veins below the tongue. But the veins which are phlebotomized in the arms are 5; because one of those is a cephalica vein, and it comes from the wild side, and the people call it “the vein of the head”; and it is hollow; and it is a small vein, and it consists of one branch of the basilica, and another of the cephalic; and they call that the people “vein of the whole body”, and the basilica is the vein which is found on the domestic side, and is called “citillar”, and the commoners call it “the vein of the belly”; and it is the funis of the arm, and is found above the elbow, and it is the one that appears prominently; and salvatella, and it is the vein between the small ear and the little finger, and in it are two branches.
And in the foot and leg there are three veins: one of these is below the curve of the jaw on the wild side; and the second is sophena, and its location is below the ankle of the domestic side; and sciatic vein, and its location is below the ankle of the wild side; and in the leg are other veins similar to those.

For if the blood is black, then you must let it out until it becomes red; and similarly, if you see that it is thick, then you must let it out until it becomes thinner; and similarly, if it is sharp, then you should stop the acuteness of it.
And for those who are full and strong and need bloodletting in one session, the phlebotomy of the vein should be enlarged and the phlebotomies should be wide.
And for those who are weak, it should be done in the contrary way; and if blood is to be extracted from them in many sessions, then the incision should be narrow.
And the best method for phlebotomy of a vein is that the section be transverse and not a puncture.
And this method of phlebotomy is safe from blood flow and from cutting of nerves, and it is more praiseworthy and safer than phlebotomy according to width and length.
And to those who are accustomed to having syncopes due to phlebotomy, you should cure it before the phlebotomy with a piece of bread soaked in wine of pomegranates or of simple oxymel warmed up; and extract from them blood in three or four sessions.

And he who wants to open his veins and dissolve his blood in the second vein, from which phlebotomy is due to great evacuation, and whose nature is cold, should evacuate blood little by little according to the quantity of its virtue on consecutive days.

But he who wants to draw blood from his body in the contrary direction of that to which it inclines, should be phlebotomized on the second or third day.

And when you want to open the veins and extract blood in the second session, if the orifice of the vein is already closed and it is difficult for the blood to flow, do not press on it with vehemence and have the extended arm slightly bent, for that is very bad.
And you should not let it be phlebotomized a second time unless the orifice of the vein is open; or else if the blood coagulates in the incision of the vein, pause over that with alfarach terychium, or with sagzenea, and compress it lightly until the blood flows out.
And if the vein is inflamed, then do not touch it until the inflammation has subsided.
And if you are compelled to evacuate blood a second time and there is no excuse, then phlebotomize it over that spot or on the other arm in another vein.
And the vein called "the strings of the arm" is phlebotomized in place of the cubital or basilic vein when they are not found or hidden, for that is composed of them.

But if you are in the summer days, then you will be excused from immersing your hand in water, and it is sufficient to have the body of blood immersed in warm water during winter time.
And phlebotomy of this vein is safer than of all other veins, because there is no pulsating vein or nerve beneath it.

Of wind-making and of the qualities of use of those.
Vents are of horns, or of ram, or of skin.
And wind-making is done according to two methods: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is wind-making without scarification.
And this wind-making which is without scarification is according to two methods, for it is with fire or without fire.
And the vents which are administered with scarification and extraction of blood are xii sites of the body, of which one in which vents are paused is alnocrati, and it is the hinder part of the head; and the middle location of him from two parts of the neck together; and vents of the chin below the mandible further down from the mouth; and vents of two spatulas; and vents of alo-hoster, and it is upon the end of the breastbone; and vents of two little holes, and they are of two arms; and vents of two ankles; and vents of two buttocks.

Thus begins the third part: on restoration. This chapter is many times necessary in the art of medicine; and it is about the restoration of fractures and accidental dislocations of bones.
Oh son, you will understand that this chapter is mocked by foolish doctors and the vulgar, and those who have not read the works of the Ancients nor understood a letter from them.
And for this reason, the manner of science in our land is hidden; and I have not been obliged to do so at any time up until now; and I have not used what I learned, but rather through long study of the works of the Ancients and understanding their wisdom, from which I extracted their knowledge.
And indeed, I have added to it through experimentation and study throughout my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that my knowledge and experience have taught me, and I have shortened it for you as much as possible so that it does not tire you out; and I have simplified it at the end of its explanation; and shown it at the end of its demonstration; and formed many forms of instruments which are administered in it, as has been done through the addition of explanations, just as I did in two previous chapters.
First chapter.
Summary and collections from the existence of fractures of bones, from which their premise is necessary, before we begin to address the healing of broken and diverse members. And therefore, we shall begin this chapter with summaries of speech; and the chapters at the understanding of which in the beginning are perhaps the artisans, and they stand on the truth of those who speak them; so may you and those who understand or learn this noble art from you.
And therefore I say that when there is a fracture or dislocation or twist or hernia, then you shall first apply phlebotomy or poultice to it, or any other treatment if no counterindication exists, as well as in debility of strength, or if it happens to an infant, or elderly person, or during hot weather or excessive rubbing.
And indeed, let them eat a meal of fresh herbs and poultry and crab, and avoid fatty meats and anything that fills the veins with blood, so as to guard against the occurrence of pus in the affected member; and if you do not fear effusion of matter into the member.
And then return the patient to their usual diet.
And indeed, when the broken bone begins to be restored, then let the patient eat nourishing foods that are light and easy to digest, such as meals made from wheat cooked with water, rice, and peas, and fish, and fresh meat, and gross foods, and anything similar which is easy to digest; and with this diet, the healing of fractures occurs more easily and gently.
And indeed, you shall know that broken bones when they are in elderly or weakened men, it is not possible for them to be continued and consolidated according to their nature due to the drying of their members.
But indeed nature adjusts and connects the broken bone of any part, similar to glue which adheres and binds together, until it comes to an end in strength and firmness, just as fire does at the beginning, so that no counterindication exists for it from its works.
And for this reason, it is necessary to restrict the patient's diet, and let them eat foods which are heavy and viscous and fatty, just as we have said.
And indeed you shall know that the species of fractures are diverse according to the variety of members, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the foot is different from a fracture of the bone of the toe; and likewise all other members are diverse among themselves.
And indeed I have remembered all species of fractures, explained in their own distinct chapters from others.
Many varieties of fractures within themselves. And the species of fractures of bones are diverse in their joints; for an arched fracture is different from one which occurs in the stars; and some times there are fractures according to the length of the bone: and indeed they are fractures of the satagium and astragali and adjacent parts, just as it happens when writing is prepared, and a fracture is fissure small.
And indeed each species has its own pain or discomfort in its healing according to the distinctness of its location.
This indicates a fracture in the bone. And by this it is known that there is a fracture of the bone; namely, when you feel its crookedness and appearance with your hand and when you press on it with your hand.
And if there is no crookedness evident in the spot, nor any discomfort when you touch it with your hand and move it, and the patient does not feel much pain, then it is not a fracture.
Sometimes it is possible that there is crookedness or a slight crack, but you should not worry about this if when you press on it with your hand and move it, the patient does not feel much pain; instead, place over it some medicines of which you have a reminder that is appropriate for the spot; and then it will be tight because of a subtle constriction.
And know that if the spot is bent and not split in two or otherwise affected so that it does not become gangrenous throughout its entire surface, then you should begin to correct it with cauterization after an hour has passed; and if anything happens to it, then a cautery should be used.
And if gangrene does occur, then let it remain for several days until the gangrene subsides; and then heal it in whatever manner you can that is suitable and effective.
And know that the restoration and piercing of it is more dangerous than the bone in which gangrene occurs.
And strengthen it according to what will be remembered about it.
But if in the bone gangrene occurs, then extension of the broken member from two parts is necessary: if it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two cords or one cord and the hand.
And hold the member in its natural position while the broken parts are extended, then you will have to return them by artifice and in whatever manner you can in their places.
And beware according to your strength that you do not cause pain or injury to the patient through your operation; and according to your ability apply one bone to another in their best forms.
And so that in that hour you may beneficially touch it with your hand, and if you see any different cause, correct it and heal it according to the amount of your skill.
And avoid violent extension and compression, for there are foolish doctors who often cause gangrene cauterization through their operation or cause contraction in the member as we have seen it many times because of their operation.
And the quality of constriction of the broken member is that which you see; for you should know that broken members are different in their smallness and in their greatness and in the shape of them.
And of those that are small like the arm and the finger and the wrist and their like, then let the wrapping be of very soft cloth.
But those that are large like the thigh or the leg, then let the wrapping be hard and laced, for the laced binding connects the large member and strengthens it all around with even constriction, so that no ulcer occurs in it.
And then begin, and then your operation of equaling, pause binding convenient to that, over the spot of that fracture with three or four wrappings according to how much the member deserves; and strengthen your hand a little with the binding.
And then go to that at the highest part of the spot of the fracture, and strengthen your hand so that you do not loosen the constriction with which you have strengthened the spot of the fracture. And then lengthen it with wrapping of the spot of the fracture a little, and loosen the constriction a little by little, while you take from it one great part.
And then take another bandage and wrap that other part over the broken spot with wrapping, and then go to the wrappings at the lowest part of the fracture; and let your operation be in the constriction of the wrapping and in loosening it according as we have said above in the first wrapping.
And then pause between the wrappings of hemp or cloth with which the crookedness of the fracture is evened out, if there is crookedness in it; and if not, then do not pause for any cause.
And then pause another bandage over those wrappings decently done as much, if in the spot there is no inflammation or gangrene cauterization, and then pause over it that which soothes the gangrene and stops the inflammation; and let it remain for several days; and then strengthen over it the splints.
And let the splints be of the thickness of a split cane that is well formed, or be of wood, or of reed, and let the width of each splint be according to this form and figure:
However, let the splint that is paused over that fracture be thicker and a little longer than the other splints, and the length of the splint according to how long it has been over the member in greatness and smallness; and then strengthen over the splints with another bandage according to your first constriction; and then lace over it with decent wire according as we have said about constriction, and let your constriction be less, and how much longer is the fracture, so much less is the constriction.
And let the threads be middling in thickness and fineness, and be of soft wire; and if the threads are thick, as we have seen by the operation of some foolish doctors who tie their threads with strings of linen, and it is a great error when the constriction is not equal.

Way of bandage as a bath for healing of fracture, convenient to many complexities and especially to women and children, for it is that over which there does not go heat nor friction: and take from powdered bran, and it is the meal of the wheat that remains after the movement of the mill in the walls of the mill.
