Method of a plaster which aids in the restoration of broken bones and when you want resolution of the residue of pustules: take ground myrrh, chamomile and violet flowers, and flour of linseed, of all these an equal part .i. Let all be strained and thickened with wine, if the member is not damaged.
But if it is damaged, then cook it in water scented with coriander or water and use it.
Another method of a very strong plaster in resolution, when pustules occur with the restoration of a member: take ground myrrh and licorice root, and caraway seeds, and melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, all these in equal parts i drachms; let all be strained and thickened with water or wine, all according to the quality of the member and its color.
But Hypocras does not dry on its own, it should be applied to the broken member for its restoration unless a poultice is made of wax and oil and nothing else; and let it be between thickness and thinness.
But it is seen that they are applied to the broken member for its restoration and the causes in which there is dryness with little heat are similarly as myrrh, onions and their likenesses of those causes which we have recounted.
Method of a plaster which is beneficial for injury and pain: take ground mace, and nuts, and human hair or bird feathers, and myrrh, and salt, all in equal parts; let them be strained and thickened, and let a plaster be made from it.
But the amount according to the quality which covers the ligature and let it be bound, is that you consider if there is no pain nor swelling nor the bone broken not drawn from its place, then do not loosen for several days. And if there occurs at the location violent pain or swelling or inflammation, then you cut it or loosen it in that hour and stop the plaster from it.
And take a ligature of linen and sponge the sea-lion which is moistened, and let that be soaked in tepid water, and with that wash the location until that swelling and the pain are stopped; and then let the member rest for an hour; and then apply to it fine wool which is saturated with sweet wine and oil or rose oil, and let it lie upon it for a night unless you are certain of pustules cauterized, and that the member's inflammation is abated and its pain relieved.
And then repeat that subtle binding and small plaster and do not tighten it by your first constriction, and let it be subtle with that until it is healed.
And if the pustules and heat and pain and inflammation and all the accidents are now abated entirely, and you have stopped using the plaster and binding, then do so as you did at the beginning equally.
And if no cause occurs in the member of those which we have mentioned, then do not loosen it but let it be bound for three or four days or five or six. and some times let it be bound for 20 days according to what you appear by the loosening of the member, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and the flesh comes together over that.
And then tighten the binding but not as in your first constriction entirely, and let there be less thickness of the bandage of the patient's body, according to what precedes its memory.
But if you see the location of the fracture dry and exhausted but not closed, then know that nourishment is prohibited from coming to it; and therefore soak it with tepid water for three days in total, and loosen the binding a little, and through this operation the member will receive nourishment, and it will be healed little by little.
But that which the restorer of the broken member does another inspection when it is not healed at the beginning according to how it was, and is healed according to crookedness, is error according to their operation and great fear, and if they were right in their free limbs and bent upon them.
And I have not seen any of them do that, because it is more reasonable that it does not happen.
Chapter according to: Of fracture when it occurs in the head.
The kinds of fractures are many, and their shapes are diverse, and the causes are distinct; for some fractures are done by blow with a club, and the blow strikes all the bone within the head, and that is under the skull, as it would be in the trunk, and through this this kind of fracture is named asial; or the blow goes into any part of the bone, and goes into the flesh of it and does not go inside to its end; and this kind of fracture is named absolute elevation.
And the amount of these two fractures is great or small, and the cause of it is blow with club or stone, and to their likenesses.
And this fracture is penetrating so that it approaches the membrane which is under the bone; or it is in the membrane of the bone, and the amount of these two fractures is great or small.
And in fracture it is split in the bone, in the thinness of the hair small, and because of that this kind of fracture is named capillary fracture.
And it is fracture that is done or by blow with stone or by blow of club, and which makes inside any lamina of the bone to its splits fingers, and it is done in the location bent so that it happens to the nails when there occurs to them blow and it is split of their splits fingers.
And this happens mostly in bodies with wet humors, as well as in children's heads.
And after all these kinds of fractures have been set, they are bound like separated stars. And then he will have a memory of everything in its place.
And know all these kinds of fractures through detection on them and through inquiry with feelers and the appearance of rotten flesh that is upon them.
And hairline fracture is known by what is uncovered on the bone, and cleaned, and felt with feelers, for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of fractures, consider first the accidents of the patient, and then if you see through the accidents that indicate by obvious sign over fear, as in vomiting of cholera, extension, loss of reason, loss of speech, fainting, acute fever, discharge from orifices, and redness of these, and their likeness to them in the major dispositions, it is not healed.
And if you see non-current accidents you expect health, then you begin his treatment.
The reason is that if the sick person came to you in the beginning as he was sick and it is in the days of winter, then consider that you study in the appearance of the bone before the day fourteen according to all dispositions.
And if it is in the days of summer, then consider that you cut off the bone before the seventh day, having that which is below the bone be rotted by the pannicle, and that the accidents we have counted occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the cerebrum, and is in small pieces and crushed, then consider that you cut off the crushed and ground-up piece, according to what I will count for you.
And it is that you raise the head of the sick person and uncover over the bone according to your custom and manner possible according to the figure of the plague, and that he is lighter on the sick person.
And if it happens to you to do the detection of the bone, blood flow or cauterized pus, then you oppose it with what is appropriate, and it is that you apply the place with cloths infused in wine and rose oil, and let the plague rest until the aposteme is calmed, and be secure from the blood flow; and then begin in the circulation of the bone and in its ablation.
And it is according to one of two manners of operation.
And the one manner is that you cut into the bone with a narrow scalpel, and this is its form: And then administer another scalpel this, a little wider than that; and this is its form:
And then administer the scalpel wider than the second.
And consider that it must be due to you a number of different scalpels, of which some are wider than others; and some of them are shorter than others and are in the last of acuity of their extremities, and they are of iron or steel.
And use them with ease in percussion on the scalpel so that it does not disturb the head and lead that one.
And if the bone is strong and hard, then consider that you bore into its circulation before you administer the scalpels with the trepans, which are named non-deepening trepans.
And they are not named thus but because those do not stop the end of the bone from it that is under it, for so that to the trepan there is a rounded extremity or margin on that which is below the head of it pointed like margin or small circle, which prohibits being submerged and passing the thickness of the bone.
And it is convenient that you take of these trepans great number of which each one suits the thickness of each bone, while it is present to you in all heads of the brain, the extremity of which is acute, the quantity in length and brevity is according to the quantity of thickness of this head.
And this is the form of three species of trepans: The quality of boring into the circulation of the broken bone is that you pause the trepan over the bone and turn it with your fingers while you know that the bone has been penetrated; and then replace the trepan at another location, and pause what is between all trauma the quantity of the thickness of the groove or environment that.
And then insert with scalpels what is between each break of the bone, and do that more subtly that you can upon its end in thinness, as we have said, while you irradiate the bone or by your hand or by any other cause by any tools which you will have apparelled for that, as tweezers and subtle forceps.
And consider that you keep with all precaution that the scalpel does not touch any cause of the brain.
And when you have circulated the bone and saw it separated from the brain if it is continued with it, and you have extracted that, consider that you raise and gnaw that in the asperity of it of what is in the bone by other instrument similar to scalpel, but however consider that it is more thin and more subtle than the other scalpels.
And if a little piece of the small bones and fragments are raised, then take that with ease by what is due to you of the instruments; and then cure the plague with poultices and ointments which we have said.
And the other manner of operation is lighter, farther from fear, which says Gualia and is praised by great praise; and this is its speech: it is necessary for you in the beginning that you begin to uncover the piece of the bone of the place in which is the fracture most violent and most manifest, while you have uncovered that piece and you have paused below it the extremity of this scalpel sluggish and gentle of which this is the form:
And that the lenticular piece is lion and does not insert any cause, and the acute piece is of it in two sides forward in length, as you see; let the lenticular piece be adjusted to the brain with the membrane of it incident acute into the bone.
And then percute upon the scalpel of one piece with a small mallet while you insert all the bone with ease as it is turned, and you are secure of the membrane that does not occur in it accident which you fear in any manner, and if the patient was the most foolish of all men and the most ignorant of them; certainly, and if he was sleeping.
Then if it raised any cause annexed to the bone of the membrane in some places of bone, insert that of it with the extremity of the scalpel that lenticular, and loosen that of it with ease, for it is loosened from it its harm and its fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate within the membrane, and what is cut off or remains of the bone is the membrane of it, and raised in it asperity and small fragments, then consider that you raise that asperity and loosen those fragments with subtle razors by which you have prepared their number, of diverse quantities so that it is convenient for you to administer them in every place most conveniently and rightly according to what you become involved in your operation on the broken bone and the figure of it.
And consider that you administer in your reason with which you raise the bone at the beginning the widest of those razors, and then use it of the most subtle one, and do not cease doing that according to continuity while you come to administer the most thin and the most subtle of all those.
May each of the other small capillary fractures and subtle fractures, that you administer care according to what you have lost for his correction.
And it is a cause that is not wiped away above all those who have studied in that art. And this on that which we written have shown in great fractures.
And if the skin is stretched when you draw out the bone, then take cloth according to the amount of the wound and soak it in rose oil and place it over the opening of the wound; and take another piece of folded or doubled cloth and soak it in wine and rose oil and place it over the first cloth and do this gently on the pus so that you do not harm the head; and then administer on it lard, and do not tighten it but according to the amount that the wound has so much only.
And let him have this treatment for a day or two until you are sure of the cauterized wound; and then loosen it and administer medicines with which there is superficial dryness, as well as lint root and wheat flour, and powder of onions and of aristology, and of like things to those.
And it is that you make of these medicines powder that is sprinkled over the wound, as they are dried; and universally, administer in that all medicine of property of which is that it cleanses and does not bite.
And beware according to your virtue that the wound in your cure be clean; and let there not be in it surdiness of ointment or other; nor let you leave to aggregate virulence in it; for when it is applied to the skin of the brain, it corrupts it and poisons it, and malignancy occurs on the patient accordingly.
And it occurs in the head, as of it is cleansed the bone and mostly when the cure of it is diminished, and there is blackness on its surface; and therefore consider then if it happens to the patient the accidents which we have said, know that they are all deadly.
And if blackness does not occur but of the medicine which is applied to it and it is in virtue of that medicine that makes that blackness, then take honey one part and rose oil parts 3. and soak those lines in cloth, and then place it on the head; and then cure that with the spices of cure which are convenient until it is healed.
Chapter .iii.
Of restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except in two places, the upper and lower, or one of those which are seen, because there are two bones in it, as the lower part is cartilaginous, and is not broken and does not occur to it except contraction and tortuosity and sinuitat.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then take your thumb and stick it in your nose, and hold that fracture with your index finger and with the tip of your thumb, within until the nose returns to its natural figure. And let this be done by you with ease, and beware that pain does not occur to the patient through your operation.
And if the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then let it be made equal with the tip of the bone in which there is a small thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do likewise.
And cure it with restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then let him have rest for the cauterized wound.
And then insert into the tip of the nose a piece of cloth if the fracture is in one part, or put in two calques if the fracture is in two parts, and let there be thickness in the calque of so much quantity that it fills the crack in the nose.
And some of the old restorators say that the calques be lined in wax, and changed every day, but I have not seen this; they have let the cloth be lined in honey comfit with powder of millings; and then leave it as the bone is firmed and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes let the nose be inserted into it, instead of the calques, a reed of hollow reed more than it is wrapped in cloth of lion; and let the fracture of the nose be more violent, and that the patient not be prohibited from inspiration.
And this cause is not necessary to you; and if you want, do it, and if you want, do calques.
And if another wound occurs, cauterize the nose with cerate or with a plaster infused in wine and rose oil, or with diaquilon.
But if it does not occur to the cauterized wound, then let you plaster that on the outside with sesame flour and onion powder mixed with honey; and then place over it a piece of lion skin, and do not tie the nose for any cause.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or are crushed, then cut them and pull them out with instruments appropriate to them; then sew up the wound, and cure it with that which causes flesh and makes firm, and plaster it with that which is convenient.
And if a wound occurs within the nose, then let you cure that with calques and administer lead canula so that it is entirely healed.
Chapter .iv.
Of restoration of the mandible when it is broken.
When the mandible is broken and its fracture does not have pus, beware then if the fracture is only outward and is not broken in two and bent inward at the parts within, then its understanding is easier.
And therefore, if the fracture is on the right part, let you insert your left index finger into the mouth of the patient; and likewise if the fracture is in the mandible on the left side, then insert your right index finger; and press it with that at the flexibility of the fracture within by your other hand from the outside of the bone; by that decently equal it.
And if the fracture of the mandible is already separated in two, then let you administer extension of two parts according to rightness so that its equalization is possible.
And if it occurs knocking of teeth with destruction, then strengthen those of which you hope will break with wire of gold or silver or silk; and then place cerate over the broken mandible and then place doubled cloth over it, and place over the cloth great star decently made, or a piece of wood alone to the length of the mandible; and then tie that above according to how you are equipped with its application so that it is not destroyed.
And command the patient that he have rest and quietness, and give him food of him sorbing cool drinks.
And if you judge that the figure is already changed in some manner, then let you hold and loosen it on the third day; then rectify what has been changed of it and plaster it with powder of millings, with honey, or sesame flour, after you have removed its cerate, and place over the plaster lion skin.
And when you have seen that and adjusted the plaster over it, and the disposition of the bone is not changed, let it be, that you do not loosen it until it is healed and the fracture made firm; for many times this fracture is tight in three weeks.
And if another boil occurs in a different place, then administer what we have said many times in the sedative while he is sleeping so that it heals and that boil goes away.
And if the fracture is with a wound, then consider whether it is a separated bone or crushed bones, then you subtly put those crushed bones in place with what is convenient for that in removing them from the tools.
And if the opening of the wound is narrow, then you widen it with bloodletting according to the quantity of your need.
Then, when you have put those crushed bones in place and if they do not heal by themselves, then you cover the opening of the wound if it is wide; and if not, place one of the dressings that are convenient for that over it until it heals.
Chapter 5.
Of the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
Many times the collarbone is broken from in front of the chest, and that fracture is done in one of three ways.
Because it is either broken, separated or separated into two parts without crushed bones; and it is easier to restore it; or it happens that there are crushed bones in it, and its restoration is difficult; or the fracture is with a wound.
And the operation on it, when the fracture is without a wound, is that you have two ministers present, so that one holds the helper and follows the broken collarbone, and the other stretches the neck towards that broken part.
Then, align the fracture with your fingers while it is done in the shape of it according to how it used to be, and there should be no small protuberance or hollow in it.
And if you need more extension, then put a pillow made of cloth or wool under the patient's shoulder, and its size should be according to the quantity of your need; and extend and elevate the collarbone and hold the pillow with your hand while you align the fracture as it used to be.
And if you cannot pull the end of the collarbone to the outside parts because it has come to the chest, then let the patient lean back on his neck, and place a cushion half in size over the muscle of it.
And the minister presses the muscle of it to the outside parts while the bone of the collarbone is elevated up towards the chest, and then you straighten the fracture and align it with your fingers.
But if you feel crushed bones in the collarbone that are not healed, then split it open and pull out those crushed bones easily.
And if it is retained in the bone, then you invent an incision of it with one of the knives that you have prepared for that purpose, after you have placed under the collarbone the tool that serves the head; and it is a wooden or iron tool of which this is the shape of it:
And if the fracture is with a wound, then invent an incision of it according to the edge of the wound, and place over it a dressing that is convenient for that; and if the wound is made in one of the parts of the bone, then you split that part with the tool.
But the length of it should be according to what is possible for you in the operation; let it have two ends, one end broad and the other narrow.
And if the opening of the wound that you have split when you have pulled out the crushed bone is wide, and there is no danger of an abscess, then you close the two lips of the wound with stitches.
And if the opening is small, or you fear an abscess, then you fill the wound with cloth and powder according to the quantity of the split in the wound.
And if an abscess occurs, then soak a cloth in warm vinegar and rose oil and place it over that.
And all of this, that the stake is not healed over the broken bone; and therefore see the patient every day, because when the binding is softened or you see the stake already healed, then you straighten that and tighten the binding.
And then place the pillow under the neck of him, and place under the shoulder on which he lies a small cushion; so that with that the helper of it is elevated from that side, and therefore the fracture of the collarbone is elevated to elevation of the muscle; or his arm is bound around the neck of him.
And do not loosen the binding unless an abscess occurs in the site or pus, within twelve days.
And then renew the dressing if you have seen the face of it to that, and adjust in the constriction and restore that and leave it until it is restored and the fracture of the collarbone is coagulated.
And it is strengthened and comforted according to the month in 28 days, and sometimes in some people it is done in less time than that.
Chapter 6.
Of the restoration of a fracture of the shoulder.
The spatula is broken at the broad end of it and not broken of it except at the edges.
And therefore when it is broken at the site of it — and it is broken in the middle end of it — and it is not healed except by touch, then according to how it is the figure of the fracture split you prepare it and reduce that according to the natural figure in every way of skill which is possible for you.
And then place over the site powder of flour with white of egg and stopa of linen; and then place doubled cloth; and then place over it a slender stake of wood according to the quantity of the spatula or more.
But if under the stake is hollow in any of the sites of the spatula, then you align that hollow with stopa of linen, and then place the stake according to equality.
And then tighten above it with a long humamate with decent constriction and with firmness of the stake until it is not healed in its site.
And consider the binding every day; and all times that it is softened, bind another time and tighten it and wind the stake if it is healed in its site.
And watch the patient on his side.
And the spatula is restored in 20 days or in .25.
And when you have completed this count, then dissolve the ligament; and know that it is of the bones whose beginning is not feared nor is their dissolution.
And if you proceed to the spot of the bone's weight and place below it the chain, then split it there, and ostler it, and do according to what we have said in the fracture of the furcula of suppuration of an abscess if it happens here.
Chapter .VII.
Of restoration of the fracture of the foot.
The foot is sometimes broken in its middle part and that hurts there; but its ends are more painful because they are crushed and broken.
And of its accidents when its middle is broken it declines to the parts within and there happens to it great pain, and difficulty in breathing, and cough, and strong vomiting and blood; and a cavity is revealed in the broken bone and not concealed from sensation.
And its restoration is that the patient is laid on his back, and place under him a cushion; and press upon his muscles, and adjust his ribs by the hands of two men, and apply to the operation and to the adjustment according to all manner that it is possible and convenient, until he returns the figure of the bone according as it should be.
And then place upon it plaster and wadding, and pause over it a board of subtle deal or of cypress, or of a wood similar to them in lightness, after you have enveloped that in cloth; and then subtilise in binding that over the broken bone so that it is not hurt; and go with lacing according to roundness to the back sometimes, and strengthen that with decent stricture; and consider the lacing every hour, and when it has been loosened, strengthen it.
And if necessity requires that the bandage be loosened or pricking, or pain, or suppuration happens in the place, then you cut and loosen that and remove the plaster, and rectify what happens for that by means of cure of it; and then reduce the plaster if you see to it the face; and adjust stricture upon him until he is healed.
Chapter .VIII.
Of restoration of the ribs when they are cracked.
Know that fracture of the ribs does not happen except in large spots which follow the back; and of their ends of the part before, it does not happen to those a contraction except by reason that they are cartilaginous. And cognition of it is not concealed from sensation on account of the inquiry of it with the fingers.
And the restoration is that you grip the fracture with the fingers according to possible manner, until he is swallowed according as it should be, and then plaster and strengthen the cracked bone with a board, if it is needed by him.
And if the fracture of the ribs is declining to the parts within, then there happens to the patient vehement pain and pricking, as well as pricking that happens to that which has been punctured, and by reason of that the bone places the veilment; and there happens to him difficulty in handling, and cough, and vomiting blood; and this is of difficult cure.
And then the phlegm is generated within it by cold things.
And of them is that which I say: place upon him viands which generate inflammation and bloating, so that the belly be swollen and be extended, and the fracture be driven to the parts without.
But we ought to avoid that it be cause to generate an abscess by suppuration, if it does not happen; and if it has happened, then help it.
And some of them say that wind should be poured upon him and that it suck with strength; and this is similar to reasoning, but indeed it is feared that the wind will throw the superfluities to the spot by weakness.
And some of them say: cover the spot with wool which has been already submerged in hot oil, and place powders between the ribs until it is filled, that the bandage be made round when you envelop it according to roundness; and then let the patient be cured for pleurisy of viand and medicine.
And if it causes vehement constriction to the patient which he does not endure, and the bone punctures him painfully, fear for the patient; then cover the spot, uncover the cracked rib, and then place upon it an instrument which conserves the head, the remembrance of which precedes; and pierce the bone with ease, and extract that.
And then adjust the two lips of the wound if it is large by sewing, and cure it with plasters until it is healed.
And if suppuration happens within, then you cut and pour powders in rose oil and place over the spot, and let the patient be cured for that by what is said about suppuration within.
And let him be laid on his side upon which he sleeps until it is healed.
Chapter .IX.
Of restoration of fracture of the spine of the back and of the neck.
Sometimes fracture happens to the bones of the neck, and they hurt there at their attachment, and similarly to the spines of the back. And if it happens to someone and you want to know if he will be healed or not, then consider, for if you see that his hands are already loosened and stupified and dead and he cannot move those nor extend nor strengthen them when you pinch them with your fingers or pierce them with a needle, and he does not feel pain in them, then know that it is not healed, and in the midst of dispositions it is mortal.
And if he feels constriction in them with the fingers and piercing, then know that the bone's nut is saved and that the patient will be cured.
And if you see the spines of the neck like that and want to know if it will be healed or not, then consider his feet.
Then if you see that they are already loosened and it happens to them what happened in the hands, and then when he lies on his back he feels windiness and urination against his will, and sometimes is laid upon his back and wants to urinate but cannot, then know that it is mortal, and so do not trouble yourself in its cure.
But if none of those happens to him, the cause is better and less distant.
And its cure is that you pierce with a syringe the suppuration of abscess which you place over the cracked spine with hot rose oil, or with balls of hellebore prepared; place upon it sometimes during the day as long as the abscess lasts.
And then place upon it one of the comforting and dry plasters, and strengthen over it with lacing; and command the patient that he take rest and tranquility and not sleep on the part from which he receives pain until he is healed.
And if suppuration happens on account of the bone's attachment or for any cause that it has been separated from him, then cover that with a bandage and hold that bone; and then adjust the two lips of the wound with sewing if it is large; and then cure it with something incarnating from the gums, until it is healed.
But if the tip of the bone of the bones is cracked and it is the end of the coccyx, then place your left finger in the hole, and grip the cracked bone with the other hand according to what is possible by second adjustment; and then place upon him plaster and a board if it needs that; and then strengthen that.
If you feel broken bones in him, then you put a splint on it and bandage it, and treat the wound with the medicine that is prescribed until it heals.
