Chapter 12.
On the restoration of a broken arm.
An arm is made up of two bones, which are called two rays; the first is small and is that which supports the finger, and the other is large and is that which is placed below the smaller one, at the part lower down.
And usually the larger ray is broken alone, or the smaller one, and seldom are they both broken together.
And when the smaller ray is broken on top, then its restoration is easier, and its healing is even more so.
And when it is broken on the bottom, the fracture is worse, and its healing is difficult.
Worse than that is when both bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the smaller ray on top, then it is convenient for the doctor, in order to restore it, to apply a small extension with ease so that it may be splinted.
But if it is the larger ray that is broken, then one must apply a more forceful extension.
And if both rays are broken together, then you must apply an even stronger extension.
And you must apply the shape of the hand for its restoration and extension spread out over a cushion, with the palm at the parts on top, above all the others, and the finger joint be lower than the others, and the patient lying on his back on a raised pillow.
And there should be a cushion beneath him for height or leaning, so that the patient does not strain himself.
Then the assistant extends the arm at its lower part with his hand or a ligament, and another assistant extends it similarly above.
And then the doctor splints the bone so that it is reduced according to the best shape possible.
And if in the setting of the bone there are protrusions, then you must push back all swelling into its place with your skill.
And if there appears in it a separate protrusion that pushes against the tendon and you do not have hope in its restoration, then you must cut it off and treat it according to what we said earlier.
But if the fracture is with gangrene, I have already written about that in this chapter, and thus take its cure.
And if there occurs an abscess after the beginning of your restoration, then you must cut a cloth with a cerate made of rose oil and white wax, and let it be in between medium and thin, and strengthen the cloth over that with subtle tightness so that the abscess gives way; then remove the cerate and apply an ointment prepared from powdered mullet with honey, and then apply splints; and let the splint that is placed over that fracture be more firm and strong.
And know that the number of arm splints are seen in greater dispositions, or let it be in one ray or both rays together.
Then apply your extension over the site of the fracture more firmly and strongly, and when you are more relaxed at the parts on top of the fracture or at its lower parts, relax the extension a little according to how it was preceded in the beginning of the chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped over the fracture should be soft and moistened with water, not hard or rough; and the thread by which it is tied should be between medium and thick, just as we have recommended.
And then examine the limb and the ligament after a few days. And if there occurs some cause for which rectification is necessary, just as it happened to the limb, and then let you bathe the limb with warm water so that that swelling ceases.
Let the limb not be bound while resting, and then return the extension.
And if the extension is already loosened and the bone is healed and similar, then rectify all that with your skill.
And consider if the food is prohibited from coming to the limb due to an excess of tightness, then loosen it a little and leave it for several days so that food comes to it; then strengthen it again.
But if the patient does not have any cause for which we mentioned earlier, then do not loosen it until after about 20 days or around that time.
Then let the patient's hand be suspended by its strength of active movement, and let him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in 30 or 32 days, or most often it is restored in 28 days, and all that is according to the disposition of the complications and the dispositions of the skill.
Chapter 13.
On the restoration of a fracture in the palm and the fingers.
Know that the palm and the knuckles of the fingers often have a fracture there, but nothing happens to them except for many protrusions.
And when there is a fracture or a tendon in the palm, then let the patient lie on his back and let him be inclined according to equality, and then apply the hand over that extended.
Then the assistant extends the broken bones, and the doctor splints them so that they are brought together with good joining.
Then let you apply ointment and bandages; if there is no abscess, then loosen that and place a splint above according to the quantity of the site, and you have already wrapped that in a cloth.
But if the fracture is in the parts of the fingers below the belly of the palm, then do likewise as for a torn cloth, and command the patient to strengthen that with his palm which is torn; then let it be strengthened with a long cloth.
And let the finger splint be with a cloth that is elevated so that the finger is applied to the palm; then strengthen it according to how you can.
But if the fracture is to the outer parts, then let you place above it a splint and another splint from the part below in the palm so that the hand may be opened and straight; then apply the extension, just as the hand is turned around between the fingers with ligaments.
And if there is a fracture to one of the finger joints, then if it is the finger joint that pushes, let it be splinted according to how you can; and then let it be strengthened with the palm, and if it wants, place on it a small straight splint so that the fracture is elevated and not bent.
If the fracture is in one of the other fingers, such as the middle, index, auricular, or little finger, then it should be wrapped and bound with the said finger; or they should all be bound according to continuity, and that is better; or place a small splint over it, as we have said regarding the toes.
And in your restoration and approach to that, consider the disposition of suppuration and opposition — through your operation — to whatever causes those, according to what is repeated in its narrative.
Chapter 14.
On the restoration of a fracture of the thighbone.
The thighbone is often broken and is revealed to the senses according to what it converts into the front and the highest pus.
And its restoration is done in such a way that the ligature is strengthened over the fracture and another ligature below the fracture, and the patient lies on his stomach and on his back.
And then the minister extends all the ligature to its end according to its quality, when the fracture is in the middle part of the bone.
But if it is near the root of the thigh, then let the ligature be near the buttock at the end of the hip, so that the extension goes up over it, and the other ligature below the fracture.
And similarly, if the break is near the buttock, then let the ligature be made near the buttock, and the extension to the parts below.
And then the doctor should join the bone with both hands until it is reduced according to its natural appearance and the bone is joined by a good conjunction; then when the member has paused plaster and constriction, if suppuration does not occur in it. But if suppuration occurs in it, then you should leave it for several days until the suppuration has subsided, and then return to your cure.
The constriction of it should be done thus: have a bandage wrapped over the fracture, a hard one, and fold it two or three times, and remove the superfluity from it; then wrap the leg so that it extends to the heel and to the root of the thigh.
And insert a long needle between the thigh and the leg near the buttock at the ends below, and let the extremities of the needle come from the highest part of both parts; then roll over the leg and the thigh whatever superfluity there is in the bandage.
And then place a splint over the thigh at the site of that fracture, and of those, place a splint over the shinbone.
And then fill the vacuities between the leg and the thigh with soft rags, so that the constriction is coagulated.
And then begin with strong constriction over the site of the fracture with three or four wrappings; and as you go further away from the ligature at the site of the fracture, your constriction should be lesser and looser and more moist.
And then extend the two extremities of the needle to the ends below so that it reaches the ankle of the foot; and strengthen both extremities of the splints on the other side so that the ligature is not loosen from its place.
And then leave the constriction over it, where it is in such a way that suppuration or apoplexy does not occur in the member or inflammation and similar to those; and if any of those things occur, then treat it with removal of the ligature, and rectify what has occurred altogether, according to what we have said many times.
But if in the bone there is a piece of the bone which has pushed out, then let you join it according to what is possible for you; and if not, then open it and remove it, and cure with that from which its memory is preceded until it is healed.
And some times this fracture of the thigh is strengthened with splints in addition to being bound as we have said regarding the leg and arm.
But however in its restoration if it is made in such a way that suppuration does not occur in the patient; and if it is restored alone, or the other leg is also bound, then it is necessary for him to limp from which it has always been.
And know that the thigh is strengthened in 40 days or a few months, or a few weeks, and all of that is according to the variety of complications and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
On the restoration of the fracture of the hip joint.
Know that the fracture of the hip joint is painful, but it occurs in many ways with tension.
But if suppuration occurs in it, then it is not just a fracture or crack in its parts, and that is with pus or blood, and over that all is felt by the senses.
And its restoration is done in such a way that what is broken in its parts is joined with the fingers within so that it is aggregated and conjoined according to what is possible by position and facility and fabrication.
And then place plaster and place over it a round splint if suppuration occurs from that; and strengthen it with constriction as is convenient.
And then consider all the dispositions which we have mentioned in other fractures, as suppuration and similar to those in which you oppose any cause of them with what is suitable for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
On the restoration of a fracture of the leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones of which one is thick and is called by the name of the leg; and the other is slender, and is called the shinbone.
And suppuration occurs in each of the kinds of fracture as it occurs in both bones of the arm, and for that reason its restoration is done in such a way, and its operation is one.
And if they are both broken, then the leg is converted into all parts.
And if only the more slender bone is broken, then the leg is converted into the front parts.
And if only the thicker bone is broken, then it is converted below; and it is from those causes that they are not bound to you.
And then administer extension and position and binding of the splints according to that joining.
But however if the fracture of the leg is a crack having many splinters, then let its extension be lesser and looser, and draw it away with your strength.
And in the leg there is some cause of operation on the arm, and it is when you join the splints and complete all your operation, then you will take pieces of wood of the poplar tree which is paused below the causes which are paused alone, and they are made a binding of white palm fibers; gather them to those to which there is a small thickness, and let it not be of the fingers, and let its length be according to the length of the leg from the buttock to the most below.
And then wrap over each one a bandage in two layers according to the length of it, and place one over the part of the leg on one side and the other on the other side, and let them be of the hip from the most below to the ankle of the foot.
And then join the two splints in three places of the two extremities and at the middle place; by which with this constriction the leg is prohibited from bending to the right and left side, and stands in good position.
And a board has already been administered according to the length of the bed, and the bed should be placed on it so that it maintains its position.
And most importantly, you must do this in a fracture, and when there is an infection as well.
And carefully consider the bed each day and study it from behind as well, and you should protect it from pustules and inflammation or other similar causes; and when one of those causes happens, then apply to it that which is appropriate for healing.
Chapter XVII.
Of the fracture of the talus and bones of the foot and toes.
The talus does not often suffer a fracture; but fractures of the bones of the foot are quite common; and fractures of the toes of the foot are rare, and they usually only suffer dislocation in the joints of their phalanges.
And if there is a fracture of the bones of the foot and you see those bones protruding upwards, then the patient should place his foot on the ground prepared in this way.
Then put your weight on that which is elevated by those bones, and press and wring them until they return to their places.
And apply plaster and a pad on top of that, and place beneath the arch of the foot a small board with two edges.
And strengthen that by applying pressure on the foot with traction, as you have wrapped it in bandages and other remedies.
And when three or four days have passed, then you can loosen the bandage, for you will find that the bones are set, or they are cracked or dislocated, and the operation in those cases is as follows.
But if any toe is cracked, then restore it, and wring it in the same way as you were instructed to restore the fingers of the hand. And put a splint on the cracked toe according to its length, and have it slightly higher than the others; then place beneath the foot that board which was recommended to you, and strengthen that by applying pressure with traction.
But if that which is cracked of the bones is two or three or more, then you put on each toe a splint of its own quantity wrapped in lion's skin, and strengthen the board at the bottom of the foot, and let it stand on one of the edges of the board so that it has good retention.
And it is convenient for you not to forget that you do not consider all the causes which we have told you about in the other fractures of accidents which we have mentioned; but apply to every accident that which is appropriate.
Chapter XVIII.
Of the fracture of the bone of the thigh and of the os of the patella and of the pubic bone of a man.
When the bone of the thigh is cracked, then make that drop into a small hole, and then tilt it towards the front at the top of the thigh, and let it be held behind.
And the midwife fills the crotch with cotton, and let it be done in the bone of him just as he hopes; and then let the woman be cut, and elevate the small thigh gradually, for all that is forgotten about when the bone is before the crotch just as he hopes and the cracked bone returns.
And then put powder on the thigh, and when he wants to urinate, let him move the crotch with ease and urinate, and reduce it and return it according to the manner in which he measured it at the beginning, and then let him return to his first powder.
And do this for seven days or around that, for that is restored.
And if you want, take an ovine bladder and strengthen it over the fracture with a straw of reed; and insert the whole bladder into his bone, and then inflate the bladder within the bone by breathing through the straw, for the fracture will return.
And then let it be filled with cotton and stay like that for a few days as we recommended so that it is healed.
And when the patellar bone of a man or woman is cracked, then administer in its restoration and piercing what we recommended for the bone of the hip.
And that which is straight is not hidden in these nearby fractures and which hardly happens to anyone, especially to one who has some study and knowledge of this my book; for that fracture indicates to you in the major dispositions the way that is direct in its restoration and in its stricture, and therefore understand well.
But in the pubic bone of a man when it is cracked, take the membrane of an ox, and put the bone in that; then put on it a covering of linen, and let it be wrapped, and then let it be left for three days or around that, and let it be healed.
Chapter XIX.
Of the restoration of the bones when they are infected.
It is convenient for him to whom a fracture occurs with infection, and especially if the bone is large like that of the thigh or of the adjutor, or similar to that, that you take care and phlebotomize it as soon as the conditions of phlebotomy testify to you as we have said.
And if the infection and swelling bleed, then you must take care to retain it in that way as you pulverize over it powdered triphytum, unless another cause is present.
And then begin the restoration of the fracture in that same day, and do not prolong it if there is no infection.
But if an abscess occurs, then you should cease the restoration of it until the ninth day, while the abscess has subsided, and do not approach it on the third day or the fourth of all, for you will cause evil accidents.
But if the cracked bone is prominent over the exposed tendon, then you must see that you reduce it and wring it with your hands with ease and with little extension.
But if extension is not prepared for you and the position of it with your hands, then you reduce that with your hands with this instrument; and this instrument is made of iron, the length of which is seven or eight digits, and the width according to the amount of the infection; and it is necessary for the physician to take three or four of these according to the amount that he requires for the cure in every kind of fracture.
And let it be round and smooth with little roughness so that it is not bent because of the compression upon that in the hour of operation.
And let it be of acute extremity whose margin is in its extremities; and let the top of it be thick, and the middle of it within the splits.
And this is its shape: [illustration]
And the top of the instrument of iron according to the shape of the bone.
So that its acute extremity be elevated and placed on the prominent extremity of the bone.
And press it into a slit so that the bone returns, and let it be wrung with some extension.
And therefore force you to wring the ends of the fracture between them, and if the end of the cracked bone is thin, and it does not take that instrument with good extension, then see that you insert the extremity of that bone so that the instrument has power of that.
But if you cannot reduce the bone at all with what we have recommended, then insert it with whatever is convenient to him of the tools of which we have spoken; or saw that with one of the saws in whatever way it is possible for you.
And then rub in that which is written on the bones of roughness and minute scars.
And when the bone is reduced, and then the patient feels great pain from being bent, then you should know that the bone has not returned to its natural place; and so do that which will bring great relief to the patient.
When you have finished the restoration which you do to the bone, then immerse a bandage in black styptic wine, and particularly if it is summer; and do not apply to the wound plaster or any other thing in which there is oil, so that no putrefaction and corruption should arise therein.
And then administer stars in the hour of your completion of the restoration of the bone, and let the wound remain uncovered until you have inserted the splinter with forceps and used bandages according to the amount of the wound.
And take all care that you do not tighten the wound with the fracture; for many times foolish doctors have done this and brought about death or illness by corrosion or putrefaction in their patients. And if its tightness is lighter, softer, and different from the tightness of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or bad, and you fear those evil accidents which we have recounted, and feel pain in the spot, then do not put stars on that, but apply a bandage of tough cloth there instead of stars and tighten it with those.
And when about a day or two days have passed and the wound has begun to heal, and pus has been engendered therein, then remove above the bandage which you had applied over that with wine, and then administer poultices, with which according to our custom we medicate wounds, thus with tetrafarmacon and similar things.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage and consider the wound every day morning and evening until it is consolidated and closed, and that the member be prepared in such a manner that the putrefaction runs from it into the parts below with ease.
If many days have passed and the wound is not consolidated nor has the pus been discharged from it, then know that here are small wounds for which you should probe the wound with a tent. And of those small wounds those which are separated and removed, those from which the member is not separated and cause pain, pierce these according to what you can do.
And if pusches or blisters occur to the wound, thus is it a phlegmon, or another kind of corruption and putrefaction; then do for each of these what befits its cure, of which there is description in its chapter.
And of that in which you should place your hope and do for yourself: when a bone is broken, it protrudes over the member thus as the bone of the thigh and of the helper, and likewise of those of the great members; and do not presume to draw it out, nor to extract it, since many times death has occurred thereby; leave that until it putrefies, for often it will fall off by itself after about 20 or 30 days. And then cure the wound if you see it; but if not, leave it.
Chapter .XX.
Of the cure of a nodule which occurs in the track of a fracture.
This nodule often occurs in the track of healing of a fracture, and more commonly it is found near joints, and it disfigures the member; and very often it prevents the member from its natural action.
Then consider if the nodule is recent and administer to that medicines which have dried thus as aloe, and fennel, and mithridate, and ser�קק, and acrasium, and similar things; in such a way that you take of those some or all, and apply them to the nodule in a poultice, and bind those around it with good tightness.
And let the tightness not be loosened for several days; and then loosen it and repeat another, until the nodule is soaked; or tighten over that a lamina of lead descendingly, since lead has property to soak up all which is swollen in the members.
But if the nodule is already turned to stone and tight, and necessitates causes osting it, then split that through the part which rises above it, and insert the superfluity and prominent, or cut that with some resors within until it is soaked; and cure that within until it is healed.
Chapter .XXI.
Of restoration of a fracture when it is restored and then that occurs to the member subtle and frail, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and then that occurs to the member subtle and frail, then that is not done except for several causes: one of which is excess of soluation of the ligament and of its binding not according as it should; or by superfluity of tightness of the ligaments within which the nourishment is prohibited from running to the member; or by excess of embrocation superfluous; or by superfluous movement not in its time; or by lack of blood in the body of the patient, and by debility of him.
And cure of that is to make the patient eat and to make his heart tender within until the blood is multiplied in him; and then administer a bath, and apply warm water and leechdoms over him.
And then apply plaster over the member, so that the plaster draws to it much nourishment; or continue embrocation of it with lukewarm water, until the nourishment runs and returns to its natural figure.
Chapter .XXII.
Of cure of broken bones when they are restored tortuously and prevent the action of them from what should be.
When it occurs to the member already restored that in its healing of tortuosity and eminence of the broken bone and nodule, and it is thus deformed in figure of the member; but yet the member is not prohibited from its natural action, then do not receive the word of him who examines that member that it is newly broken.
But already there are many foolish doctors and restorers who have done this in our land; and that operation is very blameworthy, presenting great fear the sign of which is mortal.
But if the claudication and nodule are recent, then let them be embrocated with lukewarm water in which are infused herbs which soften, thus as the leaves of alder and its bark, and mallow, and similar things; and let a poultice be made on it with mollifying emollient, thus with dill-seed oil well prepared; or that it be smeared with mucilage of alder-bark and let it be bound up with lint of linen, or with oil of ceruse, and let it be plastered with that; or that they are made into fat figures and let them be bound on with woolen threads, and similar things to the medicines which are named amerman's consolidations.
And sometimes it is dissolved by frequent gentle friction which should be with the moistened hands, and let the member have motion to every part in all hours.
But if the tortuosity is increased before and is made strong and turned to stone, and necessitates cure of it with iron, then let the pus be above dried up and the continuity of the bone be dissolved, and that which superabounds of nodule of that members by means of subtle instruments, and let facility be administered in that by solicitude and study.
Chapter 23.
Of speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of one of the joints of its place, by which it is prohibited from moving, and the shape of the member is distorted, and it causes great pain and injuries to the patient.
Therefore, when dislocation occurs, one should immediately attempt to reduce it and not delay in doing so, for if it is delayed, the place will become inflamed and much more difficult to reduce.
And for this reason, one should not allow the patient to be angry or tense during the inflammation, for many times it causes spasms and pain.
But when this happens, one should apply the flebotomy to the patient's body, and let him lie down so that he may rest the inflammation; and the member should be rubbed with warm water and oil, and then reduced with ease.
And every member should be cured with that which will bring memory back to its place.
And now I have arranged the chapters on dislocation according to what is preceded in fracture: from the upper part of the body to the lower.
Chapter 24.
Of the cure for the dislocation of the lower jaw.
So dislocated are the two jaws if they do not grind; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: either they are pushed out of their places by a little movement and are slightly softened, or they are completely dislocated and filled out, so that they are softened at the joint where saliva flows from the patient's mouth and he cannot hold it closed nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped in his speech.
But when the dislocation of him is little, then in the major dispositions it returns by itself for a little cause.
But if the dislocation is entire and complete, then it must be administered reduction of him quickly, and not delayed at all.
And it is that the minister hold the patient's head, and the doctor put his finger of one hand into the root of the dislocation of the jaw within the patient's mouth if the dislocation is on one side; or he puts his two fingers together, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the other fingers outside with which he grips; and he commands the patient to stop his jaws and relax those from any side; and the doctor grips the jaw and slides it back into its place.
And if the reduction is difficult for him, and especially if both jaws are completely dislocated, then administer evaporation with warm water and oil, so that the reduction of both is made easier and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And so when they return and are gripped, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then pause upon it powdery linen with cerotum, and let it be made of wax and rozated oil; and then bind it with ease with mild ligatures.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and his head be standing between two pillows, so that he does not grind to the right or left; and do not force him for any cause to chew anything; let his food be lean meat, so that when the pains are pushed back and the jaws are numbed, then he may chew what he will.
And that should be administered with ease, and do not work on the opening of the mouth after eating and drinking and waking, so that the jaw is numbed and healed.
And if the reduction of two jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and not returned to their places, then many times it happens that fever and constant soddeness occur, and very often the patient's belly is irritated, and very often he vomits pure cholera.
And when you see this, then know that it is perilous; and many times he dies who experiences this within seven days.
Chapter 25.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and of the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated from within through its continuation with the shoulder; and sometimes it is dislocated outward at its outer end, and that is declared by feeling.
And the restoration of it is that the patient holds his head down and extends his arms. And then grip the place with your palm with strong compression, for it will return.
And then pause upon it plaster and powdery linen and strengthen that.
But the extremity of that which has its head and is continued with that, is not dislocated on any day; then it must be reduced and gripped according to what we have said and according to what your apparatus is prepared.
And then pause upon it plaster and powdery linen and compression, and command the patient to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
And with this medicine the extremity of the muscle is reduced when it is separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
Of the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated at the end of the tendon at the lower part.
And the second is that it is dislocated at the upper part of the muscle, and that is done quickly; and it is not dislocated at the sides behind the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the front by the nerves.
And mostly it is dislocated and outside at the lower parts after the tendon, and mostly in those of which there are few meats, it is dislocated quickly; but in those in which there are many meats, it is pushed out with difficulty.
And soon after some men experience impact or injury, and their muscles become inflamed with an inflammatory swelling, and are believed to be dislocated by this.
Then when you examine him lying on his back, and then proceed with the treatment for him.
And you know that the dislocation is in the limbs when, after you touch it, it feels ticklish; so that you distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy one, for you will find a clear difference between them; and you will find a hollow in the head of the dislocated muscle and it feels ticklish when you touch its head like an egg, and the patient cannot bring his hand to his ear on that side, nor move it at all.
If it is dislocated in the foot or ankle and you feel this clearly by touching, and it isn't hidden.
And this dislocation has an easier reduction when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of it is that the minister raises the patient's hand above him; and he places his fingers below the ticklish spot, and lifts the joint with strength to its place, and the minister raises the patient's hand, and stretches it above, and he places it below because it returns more easily.
But if it doesn't return after what we've said, and so from the beginning that the dislocation occurred, many days later, then the patient should be bathed in warm water and a poultice should be administered to soften and open it as if his feet were frostbitten, with aniseed, fenugreek, and mallows in water, and it is applied.
And then the patient should be laid on his back and placed below the ticklish spot he should have a pad of wool that is halfway between being loose and tight, and then the doctor puts his heel on the sphere, and lifts the head of the muscle with strength, and pulls the patient's hand so that it isn' t pulled to the sides, and another minister holds the head of the patient so that it isn't pulled to the sides, for then it will return to its place immediately.
And if you want, reduce it in this way: and it is that you have a man present who is taller than the patient, and he stands on that side, and inserts his finger below the ticklish spot of the patient and lifts the ticklish spot of him to the sides above while the patient is suspended in air, and another minister pulls the patient's hand from the sides of his belly. But if the patient is light, then it should be suspended by another cause, to weigh it down, for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is reduced in the same way: and it is that a long pole is placed on the ground, the tip of which is round in shape, like mortar, neither thick nor thin, and then the patient is placed below it; after he's placed on top of the tip of the pole, linen sheets, and the patient stands according to the length of the pole, and then his hand is stretched to the sides from the other side with strength, for the joint returns to its place quickly.
And if it is difficult to reduce it for all the causes we' ve said, then use this treatment: and it is that you take a pole whose length is two yards, and its width is four digits, and its thickness is removed according to the size of the ticklish spot in this way:
And then on the round tip are tied linen sheets so that the patient doesn' t slide down the pole.
And then the ticklish spot of the patient is placed below it, and his hand or arm is stretched above the pole to the sides, and the pole is tied over the assistant's shoulder and arm and the end of his hand.
Then the arm is placed over a rung of a ladder according to its width, and his hand is stretched to the sides, and his other body is suspended on the other side, for the joint enters immediately.
And when the treatment of it is completed according to any manner that is possible for you, and then you place below the ticklish spot a pad of wool in equal quantity; and then apply a plaster prepared from ground moly with egg white over all the muscle, as if it were rolled over it, and then tightly press down on the ticklish spot with decent pressure; and the bandage is rolled over the plaster, and his hand is attached to his neck, and he is left for seven days, and his hand is not pulled.
And it should be abstained from food for a little while, so that the limb is more comfortable, for it is easier to heal.
And then food should be given more frequently, and exercise should be done, and if it is firm and not softened, then it is healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to moisture that occurs with it or for another cause, then cauterization should be done in three spots, as mentioned earlier in the chapter on cauterization.
And if all of that is done and then seven days later the bandage is removed and the joint isn' t firm, and the plaster and pressure are reduced on it several times, and it isn' t firm, and there are cat bites and he can't lift his arm to the sides above him, then know that its nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, are cut or stretched or softened, and know that the joint will never be placed in its place.
The reduction of that which is dislocated at the foot and ankle, and behind, is done with pushing and stretching by hands, so that it returns.
And then the other pressure is administered on it, and that treatment within so that it is healed.
And if after the healing, gravity increases on the limb and delay in its movement, then the patient should use a bath several times so that the gravity is lessened and it returns to its first nature.
Chapter 27.
On the treatment of dislocation of the elbow.
Know that the elbow joint is dislocated with difficulty and returns with difficulty; and it is dislocated to all sides, and most to the front side from the back side, and its dislocation is not hidden from you because when you bend the elbow outward or downward according to whatever shape it is dislocated in, if you adjust it, then it is clearly declared to you that the joint is concaved and can't bend the arm, nor touch with it his muscle.
And know that if you bend your elbow and reduce the dislocation immediately before it comes to an inflammatory swelling, for if it comes to an inflammatory swelling, it is difficult to reduce, and very quickly it isn' t healed at all; and especially when it is behind, for it is worse than any other kind of dislocation, and most painful, and often results in death.
And its restoration, when it is possible to reduce, is that his hand be stretched by two men, and his arm spread out, and the hands of the doctor are on the elbow and below him, and he pushes the joint with the tips of his fingers of his hands inwards, or with the edge of his palm so that it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is at the front side, then it returns as he bends his hand with his hand, so that he pierces the elbow with the edge of his palm.
If the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer violent extension and strong motion.
Two ministers should extend the arm, and two ministers should hold that joint so that it is not twisted during extension.
Then the arm should be rotated all around, since it is wrapped in a long folded cloth on their hand or ligament.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with some oil so that it is made smooth by the cause that helps in lubricating the joint with ease; and then forcefully push the joint until it returns to its place.
And then the reduction of it should be stopped on that plaster in which it was stipitated and exequated with white egg, and tightened with a decent bandage, and the arm suspended from the neck, and left for several days.
And then it should be released; and therefore if the joint is formed in its place, then you should release the ligament of it and leave that.
And if you see the joint is not stronger, then you should iterate the plaster and the ligament, and leave that for several days while it is tightened; and then release that.
But if there is a great difficulty in reducing the joint and its disposition is in motion, then administer moistening in bath and subtle friction, and compression while it is elevated; or pause over the joint all-night, since it is a place of quick movement; and then let it be bound, and leave that over the bone for the day and night. And then stop that, and put that person in a bath; and when he is sweat, then you should rub the joint with temperate friction, and then iterate over that all-night, since it is rosemary of quick movement, one time, and the second, and the third. Corruption has already occurred in the innermost lining of the brain, where there is a membrane.
And if you want, pause over that cow's udder that is wet and heated with a bowl and stretched over that; and do that several times, because that is wet and reduced to its disposition.
Chapter .XXVIII.
Of cure for dislocation of the malasse, which is of the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
Malasse of the hand sometimes is displaced, and the reduction of its dislocation is easy, and the contrary is of the other joints, but however there must be swiftness in the reduction of it in the hour in which it is displaced, lest that the place be apostoematized or that apostemation occur in it.
And the reduction of its dislocation is that you place the joint of the hand of the patient on a table and the minister extends the hand of that one, and the doctor places his hand or the palm of his hand over the eminence of the joint, and pushes it back into its place.
But however one must consider if the palm is already extended to the belly of the hand, then the patient places the back of his hand on a table against extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is previous to the back of the hand, then let the position of the hand of that one be intrinically over the table, so that the hand of the doctor presses over that eminence of the joint.
And therefore if it returns immediately, then it is good; but if not, then strengthen that with plaster yielding to apostemation and leave that; and do not iterate that, for unless one passes several days on the reduction of it.
But however the joint is bent according to its tortuosity; and not know the patient any cause except that the hand is weakened and can't strengthen any cause; then you should know that the nerves are cut or torn, and then there is no use in it except that it be strengthened with cautery near the break of it and strongly do that some cause.
Therefore when the malasse is returned, then pause over it plaster which we have recommended, and let it be left and bound for 5 days; and then let it be released and let his hand be exercised.
But if the movement of it is hindered and any difficulty occurs in it, then moisten that with warm water and with friction several times, while it is elevated.
Chapter .XXIX.
Of the cure for dislocation of the fingers.
Sometimes the fingers are dislocated completely apart.
And when any of those fingers is dislocated from the back of the palm or from its belly, then stretch the finger and push the dislocation of it with your fingers, while it returns to its place.
And then hold the tip of the finger, and suspend that from the part to which it is dislocated, and leave that for two days.
And then suspend that and extend that until straight while it is greased in that its day.
And when evening comes, loosen that according to that narration, and do not stop releasing that in the day and exercising with movement, and loosen that at night; and do that for several days until it is tight.
And make it seem like that if it is dislocated from the belly of the hand, and loosen that to that part and do with that the first operation, while it is healed.
And similarly do with that when it is dislocated at the other parts.
Chapter .XXX.
Of the cure for dislocation of the spine of the shoulder.
When it occurs to any of the spine of the shoulder or neck complete dislocation and only the spines remain in their places, then there is no cure in that, because death approaches the patient.
And sign of that is that the excretion of the patient is outside without his will, and he can't retain that; and sometimes some members of him are weakened or two arms or two legs, or one of those; or it is weakened one spine from its place, because sometimes it is weakened and sometimes the removal of those is to four parts.
Then that is already weakened at the back parts and is named dislocation.
But the cure of it is that you consider if the dislocation occurs from puerperal fever, because then in that there is no cure nor of that there is healing of all.
But in that which comes from settlement or from percussion or similarly to those, the Ancients already stated their ways of cure with prolix speech, of which the most returns with profit; but I and abbreviated that which is not excused by the amount of it, because of its proximity to my intention expose, I have expounded and formed that with diverse instrument from that by which I showed that.
And those are not healed except that which occurs in the shoulder properly by that I recommend.
And it is that the patient is extended over his face on the ground, and is extended under him instrument moist so that he isn't stretched the feet of him.
And then a straight staff is placed, fixed in the ground at the end of the wall around the head of him, and another staff in air under his feet at the other extremity of the wall.
And the minister holds the staff, and it is not stopped in a pit; and another minister holds the other according to that form.
And then wrap a bandage around the patient's head and let the servants pull firmly on it; and extend the end of the bandage to the staff, which is placed behind the patient's head, and tie that around him; and then tighten the other bandage around his hips and the servants' hands, and tie those around his legs; and then adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other staff, which is placed by his feet.
And then each servant should extend the bandage to the end of the staff, and the two staffs and the bandages in their places where they are tied should not be loosen, but rather tightened as we have said.
And the doctor should place his palms on the patient's spine and press firmly down as he returns, or he may also lie on a table with his feet elevated above him.
But if he does not return in this way, then take a table whose length and breadth is about three yards, and cut into the wall near where we said the patient was lying, a space big enough for the end of the table to fit through. Then place half of the table on the patient's spine, and have the doctor stand on the other end, and press firmly down as the spine is straightened and forced back into its proper place.
And if you wish, open the turn which is wound around his hand; and then place on the ground in front of the patient's head, two staffs whose length is about one yard, and in front of his head, another two staffs, so that there is a palm' s distance between each staff; and there should be a cut in each staff through which a rope can pass, and each staff should be firmly secured to the ground so that it does not loosen.
And there should also be a vertical staff in which the turn is wound around, and it should be secured between the two cuts in the two staffs.
And at its end there is a cut through which the staff passes, the length of which is one palm long where it is wound around; and the other two staffs are similar to this one.
Then tighten the bandages that you have wrapped around the patient' s feet and the ones around his thighs with the turn that is tied to his feet.
And then secure each turn and have a servant twist it with their hand while the doctor bends the spine as we have said.
And when the spine has been straightened and the wound covered, then apply a plaster made of honey and wax; and then place a splint made of wood whose length is three digits or more, depending on the size of the swelling, and its breadth should be as much as it takes to cover the area of the gelbositat and the upper part of the spine.
And then tie it with a bandage as we have said, and let the patient take nourishing foods until he recovers.
And if there is any swelling left in the area after the healing process is complete, then apply medicines that soften and smooth it out through the application of the table for an extended period of time as we have recommended; and a lamina of lead should also be applied to that area.
And sometimes it happens that there is swelling in the back of the spine's joints due to dislocation; and this bone, which causes great pain, does not respond well to this medicine, so be careful when using it.
E si tu voles, obre am torn le qual es revolvut am la ma; e es que tu fiques en terra deves le cap del malaute en le derier del pug dos fustz, la longitut dels quals sia d’un bras, e deves le cap de lu en le derier del pug eyssament dos fustz; e sia la longitut que es entre quascun fust un palm, e ja es fayt en quascun fust trauc en le qual corr torn, e quascun sia mot fermat en terra per so que no sia maugut.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the joint of the hip and the joint of the muscle do not become dislocated except by complete dislocation, and it does not happen to them what happens to other joints of small displacement and concavity.
And the joint of the hip is dislocated in four ways, for it is dislocated at the inner parts, or at the outer parts, or at the front parts, or at the back parts.
And most often it is dislocated at the inner parts, and seldom at the front or back parts.
And the sign of its dislocation at the inner parts is that when you move the patient's healthy leg with the sick one, it is longer and the heel is higher than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend their foot because the joint of the hip is dislocated, and there is a swelling in front of the hip joint due to cauterization, so that the head of the hip has already come here.
And the sign of dislocation at the outer parts is that its accidents are contrary to those mentioned above.
And the sign of dislocation at the front parts is that when they try to extend their leg, it does not bend at all without pain, and if they force themselves to walk, they cannot put any weight on the front part of the foot; and their urine is retained and there is swelling in front of the hip joint.
And the sign of dislocation at the back parts is that they cannot extend their leg or bend it forward due to pain; and their leg is shorter than the other one, and there is swelling in front of the hip joint.
But the reduction of this dislocation is that you consider, then, if the dislocation is old and ancient, having already been treated with the proper reduction, then it does not need treatment at all; so there should be no cauterization performed on it.
But if the dislocation is recent and one of the four types of dislocation, then you may proceed to return the joint by extending it at its inner and outer parts, and also to the right and left side, for it will quickly return on its own without needing any further treatment.
But if it does not return in this way, then you should prepare a strong servant and extend the patient's leg from its lowest part, or by his hands, or by a bandage tied around the leg above the heel; and another servant should hold the patient's hand up high so that they can press down on the heel; and another servant should tighten a pointed cautery against the root of the hip joint.
And its extension is either from the inner part to the outer part, or from the back part to the front part.
And they are all extended in one motion while the patient is lifted up by their body from the ground and hangs down.
And this type of extension is common to all four types.
And so if the dislocation returns as we have said, then all is well; but if not, then it is necessary to use any of the following remedies for its treatment.
In the reduction of dislocation, when it is located in the joints within, the patient should be laid on their side; then pause the ligaments over the root of the hip at that spot between the head of the hip and where the psoas muscle is located below; and extend the ligament upwards from the part of the iliac bone to the highest part of the furcula.
Then another assistant should strongly pull the patient's arms, press on the large part of the hip joint and extend outwards with great force, for this will return to its place.
And this species is the quickest cure through which this member is reduced compared to other species.
But if you are excused and do not obey this species of cure completely, then the two feet of the patient should be bound together with a strong ligature, lightly, around both heels and both toes.
And the length of each of its pairs should be the quantity of four fingers, and the ill leg should be extended more than the other by a quantity of two fingers.
Then let the ill leg be suspended over the head of the bed, and let it be in the middle; and its length from the ground should be the quantity of two arms.
Then command a strong young man to push down on the head of the hip joint, and let another young man suspend the ill leg, while the other young man, pushing with strength, pulls up on the hip, for the joint will return to its place quickly.
But in the reduction of dislocation, when it is located on the outside, let the patient lie on his back as we have recommended for those who have stiffness; and tighten the ligament over the ill leg properly and over its foot.
Then two beams should be placed, one under the patient's feet and another under his head; and beams that are adjusted to the middle of the beam should be placed on which dampened cloths are wrapped, so that the ill leg is not damaged by the beam being between the patient's thighs, for when the extension is made downwards, it should not be pulled.
Then each assistant should extend to his part, and the doctor with his hand will guide the reduction according to the article.
And when the dislocation is in front, then let the ill leg be extended lightly, and it is according to this narrative on the psoas muscle; and the doctor pauses the palm of his right hand over the iliac bone of the ill leg, and then tightens that with his other hand, and with that tightening, he extends that to the parts below to the part of the toe.
And if the dislocation is in the back, then let the ill leg not be extended to the parts below and it is elevated above the ground; but let it be paused on a hard cause, just as it would be if the ankle of the ill leg were dislocated outwards, according to what we have said about its setting upon two axes; and it lies on its side and the ligaments are extended, according to what we have said, not much.
And let compression be administered with a board over the place where the joint is dislocated.
Thus, when the reduction is done according to what you have — and the sign of the reduction of the leg is not hidden from you, and it is that you extend both legs from the ill leg, and when you see those equalizations and the ill leg extending its leg and expanding without excuse, then know that the member according to which it has returned — and then you adjust two thighs and pause plaster and tighten with bandage so that the joint is not damaged in either part; and let the ill leg rest for three or four days.
Then release the ligament and the plaster, and compare one leg to the other.
Then, if you see those equalizations in appearance, then know that the dislocation is already closed, and then let the ill leg go.
And if you see any cause of softening, then return and plaster that, and tighten that according to your first tightening, and leave it for three days.
Then release that and make that delay in walking on each leg for several days, while it is comforted.
Chapter .xxxii.
Of the cure of dislocation of the toe.
The toe is dislocated according to three manners, for it is dislocated on the outside, and within, and at the bottom most towards the back; and in front it is not dislocated.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you command the ill leg to apply its leg against the hip; then know that the toe is dislocated.
And the restoration of all its manners is that you make the ill leg straight, in such a way that it already extends its leg, if it can over that; adhere the toe to the hip, and pause the foot between your feet; then let someone else adhere the toe with their palms, and pull those between your fingers onto the toe with strength, and another assistant extend the foot while the toe returns to its place.
And the sign of its reduction is that its leg adheres to the hip with non-laborious elevation.
Then plaster that and make the leg adhere to the hip, and then tie those together with a bandage for three or four days, and then release those.
And do not use the ill leg for walking for several days while it is healed and comforted.
But if the reduction of the leg to you is excused, then administer strong extension with ligaments of which in the cure of the hip preceding narrative.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the cure of the dislocation of the ankle.
The ankle some times is bruised by minor removal, and some times it is dislocated according to compliment, and its dislocation is made either within or outside.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the ankle swollen proceeding from the part to which it is dislocated.
But the cure of its removal is of easy reduction, and it is extended with facility with hands and is equalized while it returns.
But in the cure of it when it is dislocated according to compliment, let the ill leg be straightened and a strong and valiant minister of the part behind the two of that in the middle place of it.
Then you with your right hand the foot of the highest part, and with your left hand the part lowest of the foot in the place of the ankle.
Then pull the foot to you with your right hand, and then with the left twice, and then extend with the left; and push the foot of the leg with the right because the leg has its work.
And do that two times according to what we have counted.
Then push the foot of the leg to the leg on the third attempt, and you are at the ankle.
Thus if it returns on one or two according to this manner, and you see the foot equalized, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation upon it, for it will return.
But if you are prohibited from reducing it with what we have recommended, then let the patient lie on his back on the ground, and spread a cloth on the ground beneath him, and place a pillow between his thighs, and you have already wrapped a cloth around him so that he does not catch cold.
Then the minister should have his thighs, and another minister should extend the foot with his hands or with a bandage that lies on the neck of the foot; and each minister should extend according to the direction of their side, and the pillow should be placed between the patient's thighs so that the body is not pulled towards the lower parts due to its extension.
Then the doctor should dislocate it with his hands, and another minister should hold the healthy thigh at the bottom, for the dislocation returns with ease.
And when the dislocation returns and you are shown the health of its reduction, then place an ointment and tighten it with strong bandages, and bind the foot to the leg.
And beware of the nerves that are behind on the instep, so that the bandage over them is not violent and does not hurt him strongly.
Then leave it for two or three days; and if the bandage softens, tighten it.
Then loosen it on the third day or fourth, and forbid the patient from walking for 40 days. But if he is forced to walk within that space, it is not safe that the dislocation is not dissolved and corrupted, and then that which does not receive cure.
And if an ulcerous wound happens to him, then you should administer in the healing of it that which we preceded in our narration, in another place of cure and brooding, until it is healed.
Chapter 34.
Of the cure of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
Let him who is dislocated be stretched with a little extension without difficulty; and that which is not difficult, before it is easier.
But if the dislocation is in some bones of the instep of the foot, then let him sit with his foot raised above the ground or on a table, and let him remain thus as if he were walking.
Then you stand on your feet, and place your foot over what is beneath the joints, and then press that with your foot with strength until it returns, and see that it is already stretched and there is no prominence in the location.
And then place below the belly of his foot a table that takes up the whole foot, which has two ends, and then tighten it with decent and firm pressure for three days; and then loosen it and let him rest from walking for several days, until he is healed and assured of recovery.
Chapter 35.
Of the kinds of dislocation that are made with a blow or with a fracture, or with any other impingement.
When one of those happens, and you persevere in the cure of it and in the restoration of him, many times death ensues.
And for that, one should not presume on the cure of him, but let him who is wise in the art, with long experience, compassionate and pity, be tardy, not hasty or presumptuous; and let him use in the beginning of medicines that yield ulcerous wounds only so much, and let the patient rely on divine judgement, unless he to whom you hope for salvation from destruction, with relief from sickness, and in which some hope appears. And so endeavor to reduce it at the beginning of the cause, before he incurs an ulcerous wound.
And so if the member returns according to our will, then administer medicine which yields ulcerous wounds and heals the plague with that which is appropriate for him of the dry ointments.
And if the dislocation is with a fracture and the bones are separated, then you persevere in their setting, and do what we have said in the simple sicknesses of that which precedes them in those locations.
And guard and avoid according to your ability that which is within the ways of fear, according to what my prediction precedes you; for that is better for you towards your Creator, and more permanent to your glory, and healthier to your true body than those in your time. Thus end the words of Albucasim perfectly, from which God be blessed. Amen.
